We study a fair allocation problem: m𝑚m items are to be allocated among n𝑛n agents. The items are indivisible, the agents have preferences over the items, and the allocation must be fair (for appropriate notions of fairness). A typical fair allocation problem thus far. But wait, there’s more. The items to be allocated are the vertices of a graph, and we have to allocate the items in such a way that the allocation is fair and each agent receives a bundle of items (i.e., a subset of vertices) that induces a subgraph with a specific “nice structure.” The fairness notions that we study are proportionality, envy-freeness and maximin share guarantee; and the nice structure that we study requires that each agent must receive a bundle of “closely related” items.

While fair division of indivisible items has been studied extensively from both economics and computational perspectives (Moulin, 2003; Aziz et al., 2022; Amanatidis et al., 2022), fair division of graphs is a relatively recent line of study, starting with the work of Bouveret et al. (2017).The classic fair division literature assumes no relationship between the items; the items are independent of each other.But there are any number of scenarios where the items to be allocated, rather than being an assortment of isolated units, are related to one another. And we can often model such relationships between the items using a graph. The items, for example, may be rooms in a large building, and the graph models adjacency between pairs of rooms; or the items may be a valuable collection of vintage postage stamps, and the graph models similarities between pairs of stamps on the basis of country, design or typography; or the items may be the topics for a course (to be divided between two professors who are planning to co-teach the course), and the graph models connections between the topics. Fair division of graphs attempts to capture precisely such settings.

While fair division of graphs has received considerable attention in recent years (Deligkas et al., 2021; Bilò et al., 2022; Greco and Scarcello, 2020; Igarashi and Peters, 2019; Bei et al., 2022), to the best of our knowledge, all work so far has focused solely on connected fair division. That is, each agent should receive a set of vertices that induces a connected subgraph.Bouveret et al. (2017), who introduced this model of fair division of graphs, considered the example of office spaces in a university being allocated to various research groups,where each research group should receive a contiguous set of offices. This scenario can easily be modelled as a fair-division-of-a graph problem where each agent must get a connected subgraph—agents represent various research groups, and the vertices and the edges of the graph respectively represent the offices and adjacency between pairs of offices. Notice that while contiguity might be useful, the “closeness” of the offices allocated to each group might be just as important. Simply demanding contiguity might leave a research group with a set of offices along a long and narrow corridor, which may not be an attractive proposition to the members of the group. In the absence of other constraints, the connectivity requirement, while desirable, might produce bundles with unwieldy topologies.In some cases, demanding connected bundles for every agent may be too stringent a requirement. For example, imagine a scenario with just one agent and two items that are isolated from each other, where the agent has a utility of 1/2121/2 for each of the two items and a total utility of 111 for the two items together. As we can see, demanding connectivity results in only one item being allocated to the agent, costing her half her total utility for the set of items.

We can also think of the connectivity requirement in the fair division of graphs as a direct adaptation of the contiguity requirement in the fair division of divisible items, i.e., the cake cutting problem where each agent should receive a contiguous piece of the cake (Stromquist, 1980; Goldberg et al., 2020). But contiguity is a singularly important consideration in the cake cutting setting, for otherwise an agent “who hopes only for a modest interval of cake may be presented instead with a countable union of crumbs” (Stromquist, 1980). That is not the case with graphs. Moreover, graphs are a rich combinatorial structure capable of modelling a variety of relationships among the items, and connectivity of the items allocated to each agent may not always be the most important consideration. There is, however, a void when it comes to our understanding of fair division of graphs under constraints other than connectivity. In this paper, we take a first step towards filling this void.

We make three contributions. (1) Propose meaningful alternatives for connectivity in the fair division of graphs. (2) Study the computational complexity of finding fair allocations under such alternative constraints, even on restricted inputs. The restrictions on the input are based on the nature of the valuation functions and the structure of the graph on the set of items. (3) Identify and exploit results and techniques from structural and algorithmic graph theory to design efficient algorithms for fair division of graphs. To that end, we propose an alternative for connectivity: We demand that each agent must receive a compact bundle of items. Through compactness, we try to capture the idea that each agent must receive a set of closely related items.

To formally define compact graphs, we introduce the following notation. For non-negative integers α𝛼\alpha and β𝛽\beta, we say that a graph G𝐺G is (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta)-compact if there exist vertices z1,z2,…,zα∈V​(G)subscript𝑧1subscript𝑧2…subscript𝑧𝛼𝑉𝐺z_{1},z_{2},\ldots,z_{\alpha}\in V(G) such that for every z∈V​(G)𝑧𝑉𝐺z\in V(G), dist​(z,zi)≤βdist𝑧subscript𝑧𝑖𝛽\text{dist}(z,z_{i})\leq\beta for some i∈[α]𝑖delimited-[]𝛼i\in[\alpha], i.e., V​(G)=⋃i=1αBG​(zi,β)𝑉𝐺superscriptsubscript𝑖1𝛼subscript𝐵𝐺subscript𝑧𝑖𝛽V(G)=\bigcup_{i=1}^{\alpha}B_{G}(z_{i},\beta).111In graph theory literature, a set of vertices {z1,z2,…,zα}⊆V​(G)subscript𝑧1subscript𝑧2…subscript𝑧𝛼𝑉𝐺\left\{{z_{1},z_{2},\ldots,z_{\alpha}}\right\}\subseteq V(G) such that V​(G)=⋃i=1αBG​(zi,β)𝑉𝐺superscriptsubscript𝑖1𝛼subscript𝐵𝐺subscript𝑧𝑖𝛽V(G)=\bigcup_{i=1}^{\alpha}B_{G}(z_{i},\beta) is often called a distance-β𝛽\beta dominating set (Haynes et al., 2020). So (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta)-compact graphs are precisely those graphs that have a distance-β𝛽\beta dominating set of size at most α𝛼\alpha. Intuitively, G𝐺G is (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta)-compact if G𝐺G can be “covered by α𝛼\alpha many balls, each of radius at most β𝛽\beta.”We also define a related class of graphs called strongly (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta)-compact graphs.We say that a graph G𝐺G is strongly (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta)-compact if there exist vertex subsets V1,V2,…,Vα⊆V​(G)subscript𝑉1subscript𝑉2…subscript𝑉𝛼𝑉𝐺V_{1},V_{2},\ldots,V_{\alpha}\subseteq V(G) such that V​(G)=⋃i=1αVi𝑉𝐺superscriptsubscript𝑖1𝛼subscript𝑉𝑖V(G)=\bigcup_{i=1}^{\alpha}V_{i} and for every i∈[α]𝑖delimited-[]𝛼i\in[\alpha] and for every z,z′∈Vi𝑧superscript𝑧′subscript𝑉𝑖z,z^{\prime}\in V_{i}, distG​(z,z′)≤βsubscriptdist𝐺𝑧superscript𝑧′𝛽\text{dist}_{G}(z,z^{\prime})\leq\beta.In this paper, we focus primarily on (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta)-compact graphs. (We consider the empty graph—the graph G𝐺G with V​(G)=E​(G)=∅𝑉𝐺𝐸𝐺V(G)=E(G)=\emptyset—to be both (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta)-compact and strongly (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta)-compact for every α,β≥0𝛼𝛽0\alpha,\beta\geq 0.)

Observe that if a graph G𝐺G is strongly (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta)-compact, then it also (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta)-compact. But the converse need not hold. For example, a star is (1,1)11(1,1)-compact, but not strongly (1,1)11(1,1)-compact. On the other hand, if G𝐺G is (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta) compact, then it is strongly (α,2​β)𝛼2𝛽(\alpha,2\beta)-compact. When β=0𝛽0\beta=0, the two definitions coincide. For example, a graph is G𝐺G is (strongly) (α,0)𝛼0(\alpha,0)-compact if and only if G𝐺G has at most α𝛼\alpha many vertices. And G𝐺G is strongly (1,1)11(1,1)-compact if and only if G𝐺G is a clique; and G𝐺G is strongly (1,β)1𝛽(1,\beta)-compact if and only if G𝐺G has diameter at most β𝛽\beta. In particular, an m𝑚m-vertex graph G𝐺G is connected if and only if G𝐺G is (strongly) (1,m−1)1𝑚1(1,m-1)-compact. Similarly, a graph G𝐺G with α𝛼\alpha connected components is strongly (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta)-compact if and only every component has diameter at most β𝛽\beta. In particular, a cluster graph with α𝛼\alpha components is strongly (α,1)𝛼1(\alpha,1)-compact; a cluster graph is a graph in which every connected component is a clique.As these examples show, the definition of (strongly) compact graphs is expansive enough to accommodate several natural restrictions on the structure of G𝐺G, including properties of graphs such as number of vertices, connectivity, bounded diameter etc.

We study fair division problems of the following type. A typical instance of our problem consists of a triplet (G,N,𝒱)𝐺𝑁𝒱(G,N,\mathcal{V}), where G𝐺G is an m𝑚m-vertex graph, N𝑁N is a set of agents with |N|=n𝑁𝑛{|{N}|}=n, and 𝒱𝒱\mathcal{V} is an n𝑛n-tuple of functions (vi)i∈Nsubscriptsubscript𝑣𝑖𝑖𝑁(v_{i})_{i\in N}, where vi:2V​(G)→ℚ≥0:subscript𝑣𝑖→superscript2𝑉𝐺subscriptℚabsent0{{v_{i}}:{2^{V(G)}}\rightarrow{\mathbb{Q}_{\geq 0}}}. For i∈N𝑖𝑁i\in N, visubscript𝑣𝑖v_{i} is called the valuation function or utility function of agent i𝑖i. We assume throughout that the valuation functions are additive (unless otherwise stated). That is, for i∈N𝑖𝑁i\in N, S⊆V​(G)𝑆𝑉𝐺S\subseteq V(G), we have vi​(S)=∑z∈Svi​({z})subscript𝑣𝑖𝑆subscript𝑧𝑆subscript𝑣𝑖𝑧v_{i}(S)=\sum_{z\in S}v_{i}(\left\{{z}\right\}) and vi​(∅)=0subscript𝑣𝑖0v_{i}(\emptyset)=0. When S={z}𝑆𝑧S=\left\{{z}\right\} is a singleton set, we omit the braces and simply write vi​(z)subscript𝑣𝑖𝑧v_{i}(z). We call the set of vertices of G𝐺G goods or items as well. Throughout the paper, we use m𝑚m for the number of items and n𝑛n for the number of agents. And we use N𝑁N or [n]delimited-[]𝑛[n] for the set of agents.

Consider (G,N,𝒱)𝐺𝑁𝒱(G,N,\mathcal{V}). An allocation is a function that assigns pairwise disjoint subsets of V​(G)𝑉𝐺V(G) to the agents. That is, an allocation is a function π:N→2V​(G):𝜋→𝑁superscript2𝑉𝐺{{\pi}:{N}\rightarrow{2^{V(G)}}} such that π​(i)∩π​(j)=∅𝜋𝑖𝜋𝑗\pi(i)\cap\pi(j)=\emptyset for distinct i,j∈N𝑖𝑗𝑁i,j\in N. Consider an allocation π𝜋\pi. We call π​(i)𝜋𝑖\pi(i) agent i𝑖i’s bundle under the allocation π𝜋\pi. We say that π​(i)𝜋𝑖\pi(i) is (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta)-compact if G​[π​(i)]𝐺delimited-[]𝜋𝑖G[\pi(i)] is (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta)-compact. And we say that an allocation π𝜋\pi is (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta)-compact if π​(i)𝜋𝑖\pi(i) is (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta)-compact for every i∈N𝑖𝑁i\in N. The definition of a strongly (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta)-compact allocation is analogous.

We consider three well-studied fairness concepts—proportionality, envy-freeness and maximin fairness. An allocation π𝜋\pi is proportional if vi​(π​(i))≥(1/n)⋅vi​(V​(G))subscript𝑣𝑖𝜋𝑖⋅1𝑛subscript𝑣𝑖𝑉𝐺v_{i}(\pi(i))\geq(1/n)\cdot v_{i}(V(G)) for every i∈N𝑖𝑁i\in N, i.e., every agent receives at least a 1/n1𝑛1/n fraction of her utility for the whole graph. And we say that π𝜋\pi is envy-free if vi​(π​(i))≥vi​(π​(j))subscript𝑣𝑖𝜋𝑖subscript𝑣𝑖𝜋𝑗v_{i}(\pi(i))\geq v_{i}(\pi(j)) for every pair of distinct agents i,j∈N𝑖𝑗𝑁i,j\in N, i.e., every agent prefers her own bundle to that of the other agents.We now define maximin fair allocations by suitably adapting the definition introduced by Budish (2011). For (G,N,𝒱)𝐺𝑁𝒱(G,N,\mathcal{V}), let Π​(G,N,𝒱)Π𝐺𝑁𝒱\Pi(G,N,\mathcal{V}) be the set of all allocations ϕ:N→2V​(G):italic-ϕ→𝑁superscript2𝑉𝐺{{\phi}:{N}\rightarrow{2^{V(G)}}}. Let Γ⊆Π​(G,N,𝒱)ΓΠ𝐺𝑁𝒱\Gamma\subseteq\Pi(G,N,\mathcal{V}) be any non-empty set of allocations. For every agent i∈N𝑖𝑁i\in N, we define the ΓΓ\Gamma-maximin share guarantee of i𝑖i, denoted by Γ−𝚖𝚖𝚜i​(G,N,𝒱)Γsubscript𝚖𝚖𝚜𝑖𝐺𝑁𝒱\Gamma\mathchar 45\relax\mathtt{mms}_{i}(G,N,\mathcal{V}), as follows:Γ−𝚖𝚖𝚜i​(G,N,𝒱)=maxπ∈Γ⁡minj∈N⁡vi​(π​(j)).Γsubscript𝚖𝚖𝚜𝑖𝐺𝑁𝒱subscript𝜋Γsubscript𝑗𝑁subscript𝑣𝑖𝜋𝑗\Gamma\mathchar 45\relax\mathtt{mms}_{i}(G,N,\mathcal{V})=\max_{\pi\in\Gamma}\min_{j\in N}v_{i}(\pi(j)).Informally, Γ−𝚖𝚖𝚜i​(G,N,𝒱)Γsubscript𝚖𝚖𝚜𝑖𝐺𝑁𝒱\Gamma\mathchar 45\relax\mathtt{mms}_{i}(G,N,\mathcal{V}) is the maximum utility agent i𝑖i could guarantee for herself if agent i𝑖i were to allocate the items, with the caveat that the allocation be in ΓΓ\Gamma, and allowed to choose only the least valued bundle for herself. We say that an allocation ϕ:N→2V​(G):italic-ϕ→𝑁superscript2𝑉𝐺{{\phi}:{N}\rightarrow{2^{V(G)}}} is ΓΓ\Gamma-maximin fair if vi​(π​(i))≥Γ−𝚖𝚖𝚜i​(G,N,𝒱)subscript𝑣𝑖𝜋𝑖Γsubscript𝚖𝚖𝚜𝑖𝐺𝑁𝒱v_{i}(\pi(i))\geq\Gamma\mathchar 45\relax\mathtt{mms}_{i}(G,N,\mathcal{V}). We are interested in ΓΓ\Gamma-maximin fair allocations for the case when ΓΓ\Gamma is the set of all (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta)-compact allocations, which we denote by Γ​((α,β)−𝚌𝚘𝚖)Γ𝛼𝛽𝚌𝚘𝚖\Gamma((\alpha,\beta)\mathchar 45\relax\mathtt{com}). For convenience, when (G,N,𝒱)𝐺𝑁𝒱(G,N,\mathcal{V}) and ΓΓ\Gamma are clear from the context, we may simply write 𝚖𝚖𝚜isubscript𝚖𝚖𝚜𝑖\mathtt{mms}_{i} instead of Γ−𝚖𝚖𝚜i​(G,N,𝒱)Γsubscript𝚖𝚖𝚜𝑖𝐺𝑁𝒱\Gamma\mathchar 45\relax\mathtt{mms}_{i}(G,N,\mathcal{V}).

We say that an allocation π𝜋\pi is complete if π𝜋\pi allocates all the vertices of G𝐺G, i.e., ⋃i∈Nπ​(i)=V​(G)subscript𝑖𝑁𝜋𝑖𝑉𝐺\bigcup_{i\in N}\pi(i)=V(G). And we say that π𝜋\pi is Pareto-optimal if there exists no allocation π′superscript𝜋′\pi^{\prime} such that vi​(π′​(i))>vi​(π​(i))subscript𝑣𝑖superscript𝜋′𝑖subscript𝑣𝑖𝜋𝑖v_{i}(\pi^{\prime}(i))>v_{i}(\pi(i)) for some i∈N𝑖𝑁i\in N and vj​(π′​(j))≥vj​(π​(j))subscript𝑣𝑗superscript𝜋′𝑗subscript𝑣𝑗𝜋𝑗v_{j}(\pi^{\prime}(j))\geq v_{j}(\pi(j)) for every j∈N∖{i}𝑗𝑁𝑖j\in N\setminus\left\{{i}\right\}.

Let α𝛼\alpha and β𝛽\beta be fixed integers. We are interested in computational problems that take an instance (G,N,𝒱)𝐺𝑁𝒱(G,N,\mathcal{V}) as input, and the question is to decide if (G,N,𝒱)𝐺𝑁𝒱(G,N,\mathcal{V}) admits an allocation that is (strongly) (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta)-compact and fair? Depending on the fairness concept, we have the following specific problems.•Prop-(α,β)𝛼𝛽({\alpha},{\beta})-Compact-FD: Does (G,N,𝒱)𝐺𝑁𝒱(G,N,\mathcal{V}) admit an allocation that is proportional and (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta)-compact?•EF-(α,β)𝛼𝛽({\alpha},{\beta})-Compact-FD: Does (G,N,𝒱)𝐺𝑁𝒱(G,N,\mathcal{V}) admit an allocation that is envy-free and (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta)-compact? As the empty allocation (where no item is allocated) is trivially envy-free, we often combine envy-freeness with efficiency constraints such as completeness or Pareto-optimality. We thus have the corresponding computational questions: Complete-EF-(α,β)𝛼𝛽({\alpha},{\beta})-Compact-FD and PO-EF-(α,β)𝛼𝛽({\alpha},{\beta})-Compact-FD, where in addition to envy-freeness and (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta)-compactness, the allocation must respectively be complete and Pareto-optimal.•MMS-(α,β)𝛼𝛽({\alpha},{\beta})-Compact-FD: Does (G,N,𝒱)𝐺𝑁𝒱(G,N,\mathcal{V}) admit an allocation that is Γ​((α,β)−𝚌𝚘𝚖)Γ𝛼𝛽𝚌𝚘𝚖\Gamma((\alpha,\beta)\mathchar 45\relax\mathtt{com})-maximin fair and (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta)-compact?We define the strongly compact variants of the problems analogously. Notice that α𝛼\alpha and β𝛽\beta are fixed constants and not part of the input.

We prove a host of hardness and algorithmic results for [𝚇]delimited-[]𝚇[\mathtt{X}]-(α,β)𝛼𝛽({\alpha},{\beta})-Compact-FD, where we use [𝚇]delimited-[]𝚇[\mathtt{X}] as a placeholder for one of the three fairness concepts discussed above. See Table 1 for a quick summary. Recall that n𝑛n is the number of agents and m𝑚m is the number of vertices in the input graph. We first discuss the polynomial time solvability versus (weak and strong) NP-hardness of the problems. These results are further divided into cases based on the choice of α𝛼\alpha and β𝛽\beta. The hardness results hold only for problems corresponding to proportionality and envy-freeness, and they all hold for additive valuations. And then we move on to our algorithmic results, specifically, FPT and XP algorithms w.r.t. a combination of parameters, including the number of agents, maximum degree and treewidth of the graph. We now list our results one by one. First, NP-hardness.1.The well-known reduction from Partition that shows the NP-hardness of checking whether a proportional allocation or an envy-free allocation exists (in the classic fair allocation setting) (Lipton et al., 2004) extends to our setting as well. We need only represent the set of items as a clique. Thus Prop-(α,β)𝛼𝛽({\alpha},{\beta})-Compact-FD, Complete-EF-(α,β)𝛼𝛽({\alpha},{\beta})-Compact-FD and PO-EF-(α,β)𝛼𝛽({\alpha},{\beta})-Compact-FD are all NP-hard for every α,β≥1𝛼𝛽1\alpha,\beta\geq 1. These results hold even when there are only two agents with identical additive valuations. Notice, however, that a reduction from Partition only implies weak NP-hardness. Also, this result does not cover the case when β=0𝛽0\beta=0.2.The case of β=0𝛽0\beta=0 warrants special attention. In this case, every agent can receive at most α𝛼\alpha items as a graph G𝐺G is (α,0)𝛼0(\alpha,0)-compact if and only if G𝐺G has at most α𝛼\alpha vertices. So we can ignore the underlying graph, and treat the problem as an instance of the classic fair division problem with the additional constraint that every agent be allocated at most α𝛼\alpha items. In this case, the complexities of the problems vary depending on the choice of α𝛼\alpha.(a)If α=1𝛼1\alpha=1, then every agent can receive at most one item. In this case, for all three fairness notions, the corresponding problems are polynomial time solvable. We can reduce each problem to a matching problem in an agents-items bipartite graph. For envy-freeness, we have the additional constraint that every agent must receive exactly one item; and the polynomial time algorithm is due to Gan et al. (2019).(b)The case of α=2𝛼2\alpha=2: The problem corresponding to proportionality, i.e., Prop-(2,0)20({2},{0})-Compact-FD, is strongly NP-hard. This is implied by a result due to Ferraioli et al. (2014). The complexity of the problems corresponding to envy-freeness and maximin fairness, however, are still open.(c)The case when α≥3𝛼3\alpha\geq 3 is covered by the next result.3.For every α≥3𝛼3\alpha\geq 3 and β≥0𝛽0\beta\geq 0, we prove that the problems corresponding to (i) proportionality, (ii) envy-freeness + completeness and (iii) envy-freeness + Pareto-optimality are all strongly NP-hard. These results hold even for edgeless graphs.Now, the algorithmic results.4.If α=1𝛼1\alpha=1 and the graph G𝐺G is a path, then for proportionality, the corresponding problem admits an algorithm with runtime 2n⋅m𝒪​(1)⋅superscript2𝑛superscript𝑚𝒪12^{n}\cdot m^{\mathcal{O}(1)}, and hence is fixed-parameter tractable (FPT) with respect to the number of agents. We also extend this result to an algorithm with runtime np⋅m𝒪​(1)⋅superscript𝑛𝑝superscript𝑚𝒪1n^{p}\cdot m^{\mathcal{O}(1)}, where p𝑝p is the number of types of agents. Two agents are of the same type if their valuations are identical. These results hold for arbitrary valuations. Our algorithms are based on a straightforward dynamic programming procedure, which is only a slight adaptation of the algorithm of (Bouveret et al., 2017, Theorem 3.3) for proportional and connected fair division. But the fact that α=1𝛼1\alpha=1 is crucial for these algorithms to work.5.For all three fairness concepts and for all values of α𝛼\alpha and β𝛽\beta, we design algorithms with runtime m𝒪​(α​n)⋅2α​n⋅Δ𝒪​(β)⋅superscript𝑚𝒪𝛼𝑛superscript2⋅𝛼𝑛superscriptΔ𝒪𝛽m^{\mathcal{O}(\alpha n)}\cdot 2^{\alpha n\cdot\Delta^{\mathcal{O}(\beta)}} for the corresponding problems, where ΔΔ\Delta is the maximum degree of G𝐺G. We thus have slicewise polynomial time (XP) algorithms parameterized by n+Δ𝑛Δn+\Delta.The algorithms follow from the simple observation that for any vertex z∈V​(G)𝑧𝑉𝐺z\in V(G), the number of vertices that are at distance at most β𝛽\beta from z𝑧z is at most Δβ+1superscriptΔ𝛽1\Delta^{\beta+1}. Therefore, we can enumerate all (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta)-compact allocations in time m𝒪​(α​n)⋅2α​n⋅Δ𝒪​(β)⋅superscript𝑚𝒪𝛼𝑛superscript2⋅𝛼𝑛superscriptΔ𝒪𝛽m^{\mathcal{O}(\alpha n)}\cdot 2^{\alpha n\cdot\Delta^{\mathcal{O}(\beta)}}.6.For all three fairness fairness concepts, we design pseudo-XP algorithms with respect to the parameter 𝗍𝗐+n𝗍𝗐𝑛{\sf tw}+n, where 𝗍𝗐𝗍𝗐{\sf tw} is the treewidth of G𝐺G. We design a single dynamic programming algorithm that works for all three fairness concepts. We assume here that the valuations are integer-valued. Let W𝑊W be the maximum value any agent has for the whole graph, i.e., W=maxi∈N⁡vi​(V​(G))𝑊subscript𝑖𝑁subscript𝑣𝑖𝑉𝐺W=\max_{i\in N}v_{i}(V(G)). Our algorithms have a runtime of (𝗍𝗐+n)𝒪​(𝗍𝗐+n)​β𝒪​(𝗍𝗐)⋅m𝒪​(α​n)⋅W𝒪​(n2)⋅superscript𝗍𝗐𝑛𝒪𝗍𝗐𝑛superscript𝛽𝒪𝗍𝗐superscript𝑚𝒪𝛼𝑛superscript𝑊𝒪superscript𝑛2({\sf tw}+n)^{\mathcal{O}({\sf tw}+n)}\beta^{\mathcal{O}({\sf tw})}\cdot m^{\mathcal{O}(\alpha n)}\cdot W^{\mathcal{O}(n^{2})}. In particular, when the valuations are polynomially bounded, we have XP algorithms. These algorithms yield a number of interesting corollaries, which we discuss below.(a)Welfare maximisation: We can use our dynamic programming procedure to compute (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta)-compact allocations that maximise welfare. For example, we can answer questions such as this: Among all the allocations that maximise the utilitarian social welfare, is there one that is also (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta)-compact?The utilitarian social welfare of an allocation π𝜋\pi is the sum of the utilities of the agents have under π𝜋\pi, i.e., ∑i∈Nvi​(π​(i))subscript𝑖𝑁subscript𝑣𝑖𝜋𝑖\sum_{i\in N}v_{i}(\pi(i)).(b)Connected fair division: As noted earlier, an m𝑚m-vertex graphs is connected if and only if it is (1,m−1)1𝑚1(1,m-1) compact. Hence our algorithms work for connected fair division as well, with a runtime of (𝗍𝗐+n)𝒪​(𝗍𝗐+n)⋅m𝒪​(𝗍𝗐+n)⋅W𝒪​(n2)⋅superscript𝗍𝗐𝑛𝒪𝗍𝗐𝑛superscript𝑚𝒪𝗍𝗐𝑛superscript𝑊𝒪superscript𝑛2({\sf tw}+n)^{\mathcal{O}({\sf tw}+n)}\cdot m^{\mathcal{O}({\sf tw}+n)}\cdot W^{\mathcal{O}(n^{2})}.(c)Implications for planar graphs (and more): On planar graphs, our algorithms run in time n𝒪​(n)⋅m𝒪​(α​n)⋅W𝒪​(n2)⋅superscript𝑛𝒪𝑛superscript𝑚𝒪𝛼𝑛superscript𝑊𝒪superscript𝑛2n^{\mathcal{O}(n)}\cdot m^{\mathcal{O}(\alpha n)}\cdot W^{\mathcal{O}(n^{2})}, i.e., a pseudo-XP algorithm parameterized by n𝑛n. To derive this result, we leverage the well-known fact that the treewidth of a planar graph is 𝒪​(D)𝒪𝐷\mathcal{O}(D), where D𝐷D is the diameter of the graph (Eppstein, 2000), and the fact that the diameter of every connected component of an (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta)-compact graph is 𝒪​(α​β)𝒪𝛼𝛽\mathcal{O}(\alpha\beta).222We note that the classes of bounded degree graphs, bounded treewidth graphs and planar graphs are pairwise-incomparable. That is, no class is contained in either of the two other classes. Hence an algorithm for problems restricted to one of these classes does not automatically imply a similar result for the other classes. In particular, the reliance on bounding treewidth by a function of the diameter is crucial for deriving our algorithm on planar graphs. In fact, such a runtime is possible not just on planar graphs, but on all graphs whose treewidth is bounded by a function of its diameter. In particular, if ℱℱ\mathcal{F} is a minor-closed family of graphs and ℱℱ\mathcal{F} excludes an apex-graph, then on any graph G∈𝒢𝐺𝒢G\in\mathcal{G}, our algorithms run in time f​(n)⋅m𝒪​(α​n)​W𝒪​(n2)⋅𝑓𝑛superscript𝑚𝒪𝛼𝑛superscript𝑊𝒪superscript𝑛2f(n)\cdot m^{\mathcal{O}(\alpha n)}W^{\mathcal{O}(n^{2})}. This follows from a deep result in structural graph theory due to Eppstein (2000), who characterised all minor-closed graph classes that have the “diameter-treewidth property.”333A graph G𝐺G is an apex graph if G−v𝐺𝑣G-v is planar for some v∈V​(G)𝑣𝑉𝐺v\in V(G). That is, apex graphs are “planar plus 1 vertex” graphs. Consider a family of graphs ℱℱ\mathcal{F}. We say that ℱℱ\mathcal{F} has the diameter-treewidth property if there exists a function f:ℝ→ℝ:𝑓→ℝℝ{{f}:{\mathbb{R}}\rightarrow{\mathbb{R}}} such that 𝗍𝗐​(G)≤f​(diam​(G))𝗍𝗐𝐺𝑓diam𝐺{\sf tw}(G)\leq f(\text{diam}(G)) for every G∈ℱ𝐺ℱG\in\mathcal{F}. Notice that the function f𝑓f depends only on the family ℱℱ\mathcal{F}. Eppstein (2000) proved that if ℱℱ\mathcal{F} is a minor closed family of graphs, then ℱℱ\mathcal{F} has the diameter-treewidth property if and only if ℱℱ\mathcal{F} excludes an apex graph. For more details, see (Eppstein, 2000) or (Demaine and Hajiaghayi, 2004).

All our hardness results mentioned above hold for the strongly compact variants of the respective problems as well. So do (i) our algorithm for proportional allocations on paths when α=1𝛼1\alpha=1 and (ii) the XP algorithms parameterized by n+Δ𝑛Δn+\Delta. In addition, we show that for proportionality and for envy-freeness + completeness, the strongly compact variants of the corresponding problems are strongly NP-hard for every α,β≥1𝛼𝛽1\alpha,\beta\geq 1. (Notice that our strong NP-hardness results for the compact variants only cover the case of α≥3𝛼3\alpha\geq 3.)

The runtimes of the algorithmic results discussed above have an exponential dependence on n𝑛n (the number of agents). But this need not be a disqualifying factor as several fair division settings only have a constant number of agents, and often just two agents. Common examples of 2-agent settings, as noted by Plaut and Roughgarden (2020), include divorce settlements, inheritance division and international border disputes. In fact, 2-agent setting is one of the most intensively studied special cases in fair division; see, for example, (Brams and Fishburn, 2000; Brams et al., 2022). We would also like to point out that the exponential dependence of the runtime of our treewidth-based algorithm on both n𝑛n and 𝗍𝗐𝗍𝗐{\sf tw} is also essential; notice that our NP-hardness results for proportional or envy-free, compact allocations when α≥3𝛼3\alpha\geq 3 hold for graphs of treewidth zero (even when the valuations are additive and polynomially bounded). The runtime of our treewdith-based algorithm depends also on W𝑊W, the maximum valuation of an agent for the whole graph. This may be inevitable as well.As shown by Igarashi and Peters (2019) in the context of connected fair division, unless P=NPPNP\textsf{P}=\textsf{NP}, there is no polynomial time algorithm that finds a Pareto-optimal allocation even when the valuations are binary and additive and the graph is a tree with bounded pathwidth or bounded diameter. We believe that a similar result should hold for compact allocations as well. Also, as noted above, we can use our algorithms to find welfare-maximising allocations. Our algorithms can thus compute allocations with three properties: fairness, compactness and economic efficiency. We would like to emphasise that for algorithms that find efficient allocations, “pseudo” runtimes are rather common; see, for example Barman et al. (2018); Aziz et al. (2023).

We would like to add a word about the parameterizations that we use in our algorithmic results. As noted above, in many fair allocation problems, the number of agents is small, and therefore, parameterizations by the number of agents or agent types are quite reasonable. In particular, such parameterizations have been used by Bouveret et al. (2017) and Deligkas et al. (2021) in the context of connected fair division of graphs. Now, about the the graph parameters that we use. It is quite common in algorithmic graph theory to leverage structural parameters of the input graph (maximum degree, degeneracy, treewidth etc.) to design efficient algorithms. The two graph parameters that we use—maximum degree and treewidth—have been leveraged this way in the parameterized algorithms literature. Maximum degree, while being a simple and reasonable parameter, is perhaps less interesting because a number of problems remain NP-hard on graphs of constant degree. But treewidth is a completely different story. It is considered as a natural measure of “sparsity” of the graph, and has been studied extensively.Treewidth is related to a number of other graph parameters, and it is the source of several deep theorems in graph theory. See, for example, the Festschrift edited by Fomin et al. (2020) for the many applications of treewidth in algorithm design. We must also add that connected fair division of graphs has previously been studied on special graphs such as paths, stars, trees, cycles etc., which are all graphs of treewidth 1 or 2 (Bouveret et al., 2017; Lonc and Truszczynski, 2018). Also, parameters such as treewidth and cliquewidth have been used as structural parameters in connected fair division (Deligkas et al., 2021). We discuss some of these works below. So it is very much in line with the existing literature to use treewidth as a parameter to design algorithms. In particular, one of our goals was to apply results and techniques from graph theory literature to problems in fair division. And for this purpose, treewidth is arguably the most suitable choice.

Modern fair division literature goes back at least to the pioneering work of Steinhaus (1948). For an overview of the relevant literature, we refer the reader to the surveys by Lindner and Rothe (2016) on the divisible goods setting; and by Aziz et al. (2022) or Amanatidis et al. (2022) on the indivisible goods setting.

There is, in particular, a large volume of literature on fair division under connectivity constraints.As for the cake cutting setting (i.e., divisible goods), Stromquist (1980) proved that an envy-free allocation of the cake into connected pieces exists, but there is no finite protocol that can find such an allocation (Stromquist, 2008).Coming to the indivisible goods setting, the work of Bouveret et al. (2017), which formally introduced this line of study, contains several algorithmic and hardness results for the connected fair division of a graph. They showed that finding a connected allocation that is envy-free or proportional is NP-hard, even when the graph is a path. On the other hand, a maximin allocation always exists and can be computed in polynomial time if the graph is a tree. They also designed several FPT and XP algorithms with respect to parameters such as number of agents and number of agent types, for special cases of the graph. This was followed by the work of Lonc and Truszczynski (2018), who studied the existence and complexity of maximin fair allocations on cycles. Among other results, they proved that when there are only three agents, there exists an allocation that guarantees each agent a constant fraction of her maximin share. Most related to our work is that of Deligkas et al. (2021), who undertook an in-depth study of the parameterized complexity of connected fair division. They established a host of hardness and algorithmic results—under various combinations of parameters such as the number of agents, treewidth, cliquewidth etc.—with respect to fairness concepts such as proportionality, envy-freeness, EF1 and EFX. A number of recent works deal with the special case when the graph is a path, under various fairness notions. In particular, Bilò et al. (2022) studied the existence and complexity of EF1 and EF2 allocations; Igarashi (2022) studied EF1 allocations and showed that EF1 allocations always exists for any number of agents with monotone valuations; Suksompong (2019) studied approximate guarantees for proportionality, envy-freeness and equitability; and Misra et al. (2021) studied the complexity of finding allocations that are equitable up to one item (EQ1) combined with economic efficiency notions such as Pareto-optimality, non-wastefulness etc.In addition to these works that mainly deal with the existence or computational questions associated with connected fair division, Bei et al. (2022) studied the price of connectivity—the loss incurred by agents when the connectivity constraint is imposed—for maximin fair allocations. Their techniques included the application of several graph theoretic tools and concepts to fair division.

Much of the literature on fair division under restricted input settings focus on restrictions on valuations, number of agents or relaxations of fairness notions. To the best of our knowledge, there are only a handful of works that consider restrictions on the structure of bundles allocated to agents (other than connected fair division in the context of graphs). The typical restriction on bundles involves what can be called cardinality constraints. The canonical example is the house allocation problem, where each agent must receive exactly one item.  Gan et al. (2019) studied envy-freeness and Kamiyama et al. (2021) studied proportionality and equitability in the house allocation setting. Ferraioli et al. (2014) et al. studied the problem of maximising egalitarian welfare, under the additional constraint that each agent be allocated exactly k𝑘k items. Biswas and Barman (2018) introduced a more general variant of cardinality constraints: the items are partitioned into groups and there is a cap on each group’s contribution to any agent’s bundle. And they showed that a 1/3131/3-approximate maximin fair allocation can be computed efficiently; Hummel and Hetland (2022) recently improved the approximation guarantee to 1/2121/2. See the survey by Suksompong (2021) for a comprehensive discussion of various constraints in fair division.

We use this section only to collect all the notation and terminology we use in one place. Some of the terms that we define here will be used only in Section 6.

We now define an annotated version of a partition called a rooted partition. A rooted partition 𝒫𝒫\mathcal{P} is nothing but a partition, but each block P𝑃P of 𝒫𝒫\mathcal{P} has a designated element, which we call the root of P𝑃P. Formally, a rooted partition of S𝑆S is an ordered pair (𝒫,R)𝒫𝑅(\mathcal{P},R) such that 𝒫𝒫\mathcal{P} is a partition of S𝑆S, R⊆S𝑅𝑆R\subseteq S with |R|=|𝒫|𝑅𝒫{|{R}|}={|{\mathcal{P}}|} and for each block P𝑃P of 𝒫𝒫\mathcal{P}, |P∩R|=1𝑃𝑅1{|{P\cap R}|}=1; we call the unique element of P∩R𝑃𝑅P\cap R the root of P𝑃P.

All graphs is this paper are undirected.For a graph G𝐺G, V​(G)𝑉𝐺V(G) and E​(G)𝐸𝐺E(G) respectively denote the vertex set and edge set of G𝐺G.Consider a graph G𝐺G.For a path P𝑃P in G𝐺G, the length of P𝑃P is the number of edges in P𝑃P.For vertices z,z′∈V​(G)𝑧superscript𝑧′𝑉𝐺z,z^{\prime}\in V(G), the distance between z𝑧z and z′superscript𝑧′z^{\prime} in G𝐺G, denoted by distG​(z,z′)subscriptdist𝐺𝑧superscript𝑧′\text{dist}_{G}(z,z^{\prime}), is the length of a shortest path between z𝑧z and z′superscript𝑧′z^{\prime}.If z=z′𝑧superscript𝑧′z=z^{\prime}, then distG​(z,z′)=0subscriptdist𝐺𝑧superscript𝑧′0\text{dist}_{G}(z,z^{\prime})=0.For a vertex z∈V​(G)𝑧𝑉𝐺z\in V(G) and a non-negative integer β𝛽\beta, we use B​(z,β)𝐵𝑧𝛽B(z,\beta) to denote the set of all vertices in G𝐺G that are at distance at most β𝛽\beta from z𝑧z, i.e., BG​(z,β)={z′∈V​(G)|distG​(z,z′)≤β}subscript𝐵𝐺𝑧𝛽conditional-setsuperscript𝑧′𝑉𝐺subscriptdist𝐺𝑧superscript𝑧′𝛽B_{G}(z,\beta)=\{z^{\prime}\in V(G)~{}|~{}\text{dist}_{G}(z,z^{\prime})\leq\beta\}.(We may omit the subscript G𝐺G when the graph is clear from the context.)The diameter of G𝐺G, denoted by diam​(G)diam𝐺\text{diam}(G), is the maximum distance between any pair of vertices in G𝐺G, i.e., diam​(G)=maxz,z′∈V​(G)⁡dist​(z,z′)diam𝐺subscript𝑧superscript𝑧′𝑉𝐺dist𝑧superscript𝑧′\text{diam}(G)=\max_{z,z^{\prime}\in V(G)}\text{dist}(z,z^{\prime}). For a graph G𝐺G, a subgraph H𝐻H of G𝐺G is called a spanning subgraph if V​(H)=V​(G)𝑉𝐻𝑉𝐺V(H)=V(G). An acyclic spanning subgraph is called a spanning forest.A tree is a connected, acyclic graph. A rooted tree T𝑇T is a tree in which we designate exacly one vertex x∈V​(T)𝑥𝑉𝑇x\in V(T) as the “root” of T𝑇T; and we say that T𝑇T is rooted at x𝑥x. For a rooted tree T𝑇T and z∈V​(T)𝑧𝑉𝑇z\in V(T), by the sub-tree of T𝑇T rooted at z𝑧z, we mean the sub-tree of T𝑇T induced by z𝑧z and all descendants of z𝑧z, and we root the sub-tree at z𝑧z.A rooted forest is a graph in which each connected component is a rooted tree. For a rooted forest G𝐺G, by the set of roots of G𝐺G, we mean the set R⊆V​(G)𝑅𝑉𝐺R\subseteq V(G) that consists of the root of each connected component of G𝐺G. For a connected graph G𝐺G and a vertex z∈V​(G)𝑧𝑉𝐺z\in V(G), a BFS tree of G𝐺G rooted at z𝑧z is a spanning tree of G𝐺G, produced by a breadth-first search traversal of G𝐺G, starting at z𝑧z. If H𝐻H is a BFS tree of G𝐺G rooted at z𝑧z, then for every z′∈V​(G)superscript𝑧′𝑉𝐺z^{\prime}\in V(G), we have distG​(z,z′)=distH​(z,z′)subscriptdist𝐺𝑧superscript𝑧′subscriptdist𝐻𝑧superscript𝑧′\text{dist}_{G}(z,z^{\prime})=\text{dist}_{H}(z,z^{\prime}).

For graphs G1subscript𝐺1G_{1} and G2subscript𝐺2G_{2} with V​(G1)=V​(G2)𝑉subscript𝐺1𝑉subscript𝐺2V(G_{1})=V(G_{2}), by the merge of G1subscript𝐺1G_{1} and G2subscript𝐺2G_{2}, denoted by 𝚖𝚎𝚛𝚐𝚎​(G1,G2)𝚖𝚎𝚛𝚐𝚎subscript𝐺1subscript𝐺2\mathtt{merge}(G_{1},G_{2}) we mean the (multi)graph G𝐺G obtained by the union of G1subscript𝐺1G_{1} and G2subscript𝐺2G_{2}. And if z​z′∈E​(G1)𝑧superscript𝑧′𝐸subscript𝐺1zz^{\prime}\in E(G_{1}) and z​z′∈E​(G2)𝑧superscript𝑧′𝐸subscript𝐺2zz^{\prime}\in E(G_{2}), then G𝐺G contains two parallel edges between z𝑧z and z′superscript𝑧′z^{\prime}.

For a graph G𝐺G and a non-empty set Z⊆V​(G)𝑍𝑉𝐺Z\subseteq V(G), by the partition of Z𝑍Z into the connected components of G𝐺G, we mean the partition 𝒫𝒫\mathcal{P} of Z𝑍Z such that for z,z′∈Z𝑧superscript𝑧′𝑍z,z^{\prime}\in Z, z𝑧z and z′superscript𝑧′z^{\prime} are in the same block of 𝒫𝒫\mathcal{P} if and only if they are in the same connected component of G𝐺G.

For a non-empty set S𝑆S and a partition 𝒫𝒫\mathcal{P} of S𝑆S, a graphical representation of 𝒫𝒫\mathcal{P}, denoted by 𝙶​(𝒫)𝙶𝒫\mathtt{G}(\mathcal{P}), is an acyclic graph with vertex set S𝑆S such that 𝒫𝒫\mathcal{P} is precisely the partition of S𝑆S into the connected components of 𝙶​(𝒫)𝙶𝒫\mathtt{G}(\mathcal{P}). That is, each block of 𝒫𝒫\mathcal{P} corresponds to a connected component of 𝙶​(𝒫)𝙶𝒫\mathtt{G}(\mathcal{P}) and vice versa; and since 𝙶​(𝒫)𝙶𝒫\mathtt{G}(\mathcal{P}) is acyclic, each connected component is a tree.Notice that 𝙶​(𝒫)𝙶𝒫\mathtt{G}(\mathcal{P}) need not be unique. So by 𝙶​(𝒫)𝙶𝒫\mathtt{G}(\mathcal{P}), we only mean any fixed (arbitrarily chosen) acyclic graph that satisfies the required property.

We now make the following observation about the complexity of checking if a given partition is an acyclic join.

We use standard terminology from parameterized complexity, for which we refer the reader to the book by Cygan et al. (2015). A typical parameterized problem on graphs consists of an m𝑚m-vertex graph G𝐺G and a non-negative integer k𝑘k as input, and we have to decide if (G,k)𝐺𝑘(G,k) satisfies a fixed property. The integer k𝑘k may be given explicitly as part of the input, or it may be a function of the input graph G𝐺G. We call k𝑘k the parameter, and say that the problem is parameterized by k𝑘k. We say that the problem is fixed-parameter tractable (or belongs to the class FPT) if it admits an algorithm running in time f​(k)​m𝒪​(1)𝑓𝑘superscript𝑚𝒪1f(k)m^{\mathcal{O}(1)}, for some computable function f𝑓f. We also call an algorithm with such a runtime an FPT algorithm. And we say that the problem belongs to the class XP (slicewise polynomial time) if it admits an algorithm running in time f​(k)​mg​(k)𝑓𝑘superscript𝑚𝑔𝑘f(k)m^{g(k)} for some computable functions f𝑓f and g𝑔g.

Consider a graph G𝐺G. A tree decomposition of G𝐺G is a pair (T,{Xt|t∈V​(T)})𝑇conditional-setsubscript𝑋𝑡𝑡𝑉𝑇(T,\{X_{t}~{}|~{}t\in V(T)\}), where T𝑇T is a tree and every node t∈V​(T)𝑡𝑉𝑇t\in V(T) has a set Xt⊆V​(G)subscript𝑋𝑡𝑉𝐺X_{t}\subseteq V(G) associated with it, such that (T,{Xt|t∈V​(T)})𝑇conditional-setsubscript𝑋𝑡𝑡𝑉𝑇(T,\{X_{t}~{}|~{}t\in V(T)\}) satisfies the following conditions. (a) ⋃t∈V​(T)Xt=V​(G)subscript𝑡𝑉𝑇subscript𝑋𝑡𝑉𝐺\bigcup_{t\in V(T)}X_{t}=V(G).(b) For every edge u​v∈E​(G)𝑢𝑣𝐸𝐺uv\in E(G), there exists a node t∈V​(T)𝑡𝑉𝑇t\in V(T) such that u,v∈Xt𝑢𝑣subscript𝑋𝑡u,v\in X_{t}. (c) For every u∈V​(G)𝑢𝑉𝐺u\in V(G), the set {t∈V​(T):u∈Xt}conditional-set𝑡𝑉𝑇𝑢subscript𝑋𝑡\{t\in V(T):u\in X_{t}\} induces a connected subtree of T𝑇T.We call each Xtsubscript𝑋𝑡X_{t} a bag.The width of a tree decomposition (T,{Xt|t∈V​(T)})𝑇conditional-setsubscript𝑋𝑡𝑡𝑉𝑇(T,\left\{{X_{t}~{}|~{}t\in V(T)}\right\}) is maxt⁡|Xt|−1subscript𝑡subscript𝑋𝑡1\max_{t}{|{X_{t}}|}-1. The treewidth of a graph G𝐺G, denoted by 𝗍𝗐​(G)𝗍𝗐𝐺{\sf tw}(G) is the minimum width of a tree decomposition G𝐺G. When G𝐺G is clear from the context, we simply write 𝗍𝗐𝗍𝗐{\sf tw}.When talking about a tree decomposition (T,{Xt|t∈V​(T)})𝑇conditional-setsubscript𝑋𝑡𝑡𝑉𝑇(T,\left\{{X_{t}~{}|~{}t\in V(T)}\right\}) of a graph G𝐺G, to avoid any confusion, we follow the common practice of referring to the vertices of T𝑇T as nodes, and reserve the term vertex exclusively for the vertices of G𝐺G.

We now define a particular kind of tree decompositions called nice tree decompositions, which come in handy while designing algorithms. A tree decomposition (T,{Xt|t∈V​(T)})𝑇conditional-setsubscript𝑋𝑡𝑡𝑉𝑇(T,\left\{{X_{t}~{}|~{}t\in V(T)}\right\}) of a graph G𝐺G is said to be a nice tree decomposition if it has the following properties. (a) T𝑇T is rooted tree. (b) If t∈V​(T)𝑡𝑉𝑇t\in V(T) is the root or a leaf, then Xt=∅subscript𝑋𝑡X_{t}=\emptyset. (c) Every non-leaf node t𝑡t of T𝑇T is one of the following four types: (c.1) introduce vertex node: t𝑡t has exactly one child, say t′superscript𝑡′t^{\prime}, and Xt=Xt′∪{z}subscript𝑋𝑡subscript𝑋superscript𝑡′𝑧X_{t}=X_{t^{\prime}}\cup\left\{{z}\right\} for some z∉Xt′𝑧subscript𝑋superscript𝑡′z\notin X_{t^{\prime}}; (c.2) forget node: t𝑡t has exactly one child, say t′superscript𝑡′t^{\prime}, and Xt=Xt′∖{z}subscript𝑋𝑡subscript𝑋superscript𝑡′𝑧X_{t}=X_{t^{\prime}}\setminus\left\{{z}\right\} for some z∈Xt′𝑧subscript𝑋superscript𝑡′z\in X_{t^{\prime}}; (c.3) introduce edge node: t𝑡t has exactly one child, say t′superscript𝑡′t^{\prime}, and Xt=Xt′subscript𝑋𝑡subscript𝑋superscript𝑡′X_{t}=X_{t^{\prime}}, and for some edge z​z′∈E​(G)𝑧superscript𝑧′𝐸𝐺zz^{\prime}\in E(G) with z,z′∈Xt𝑧superscript𝑧′subscript𝑋𝑡z,z^{\prime}\in X_{t}, the node t𝑡t is labelled with the edge z​z′𝑧superscript𝑧′zz^{\prime}. (c.4) join node: t𝑡t has exactly two children, say t′superscript𝑡′t^{\prime} and t′′superscript𝑡′′t^{\prime\prime}, and Xt=Xt′=Xt′′subscript𝑋𝑡subscript𝑋superscript𝑡′subscript𝑋superscript𝑡′′X_{t}=X_{t^{\prime}}=X_{t^{\prime\prime}}.

Consider a graph G𝐺G, a nice tree decomposition (T,{Xt|t∈V​(T)})𝑇conditional-setsubscript𝑋𝑡𝑡𝑉𝑇(T,\{X_{t}~{}|~{}t\in V(T)\}) of G𝐺G and two nodes t,t′∈V​(T)𝑡superscript𝑡′𝑉𝑇t,t^{\prime}\in V(T), where t′superscript𝑡′t^{\prime} is the unique child of t𝑡t. If t𝑡t is an introduce node with Xt=Xt′∪{z}subscript𝑋𝑡subscript𝑋superscript𝑡′𝑧X_{t}=X_{t^{\prime}}\cup\left\{{z}\right\}, then we say that the vertex z𝑧z is introduced at t𝑡t or that t𝑡t introduces the vertex z𝑧z. Similarly, if t𝑡t is an introduce edge node labelled with an edge z​z′∈E​(G)𝑧superscript𝑧′𝐸𝐺zz^{\prime}\in E(G), then we say that z​z′𝑧superscript𝑧′zz^{\prime} is introduced at t𝑡t or that t𝑡t introduces z​z′𝑧superscript𝑧′zz^{\prime}. We assume throughout without loss of generality that in a nice tree decomposition, every vertex of G𝐺G is introduced at exactly one node of T𝑇T and forgotten at exactly one node of T𝑇T; and that every edge of G𝐺G is introduced at exactly one node of T𝑇T. For a node t𝑡t, we use Gtsubscript𝐺𝑡G_{t} to denote the subgraph of G𝐺G that consists of all the vertices and edges introduced in the subtree rooted at t𝑡t.When designing an algorithm for a problem parameterized by treewidth, we assume that a nice tree decomposition of G𝐺G of width 𝗍𝗐​(G)𝗍𝗐𝐺{\sf tw}(G) is also given as part of the input. This assumption is harmless because given an m𝑚m-vertex graph G𝐺G, we can compute a nice tree decomposition of G𝐺G of width 𝒪​(𝗍𝗐​(G))𝒪𝗍𝗐𝐺\mathcal{O}({\sf tw}(G)) in time 2𝒪​(𝗍𝗐​(G))​m𝒪​(1)superscript2𝒪𝗍𝗐𝐺superscript𝑚𝒪12^{\mathcal{O}({\sf tw}(G))}m^{\mathcal{O}(1)} (Bodlaender et al., 2016).

In this section, we record a few straightforward results that more or less follow from the definition of (strongly) (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta)-compact graphs. We will use some of these results in Sections 4 and 6. Specifically, results from Sections 3.4, 3.5 and 3.6 will be used in later sections. But Sections 3.1, 3.2 and 3.3 are standalone sections. These sections respectively discuss the weak NP-hardness of [𝚇]delimited-[]𝚇[\mathtt{X}]-(α,β)𝛼𝛽({\alpha},{\beta})-Compact-FD, the XP algorithm for [𝚇]delimited-[]𝚇[\mathtt{X}]-(α,β)𝛼𝛽({\alpha},{\beta})-Compact-FD parameterized by the number of agents and the maximum degree of G𝐺G, and the polynomial time algorithms for [𝚇]delimited-[]𝚇[\mathtt{X}]-(1,0)10({1},{0})-Compact-FD.

We show the NP-hardness of deciding whether a proportional or envy-free (strongly) (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta)-compact allocation exists.We note that the well-known reduction from Partition (Lipton et al., 2004) that shows the NP-hardness of Prop-FD and EF-FD can be extended to our setting as well. We just need to represent the set of items as a clique. For the sake of completeness, we state the result and sketch its proof.

To prove NP-hardness, we reduce from the Partition problem, in which the input consists of m𝑚m non-negative integers x1,x2,…,xmsubscript𝑥1subscript𝑥2…subscript𝑥𝑚x_{1},x_{2},\ldots,x_{m} such that ∑j∈[m]xj=2​Wsubscript𝑗delimited-[]𝑚subscript𝑥𝑗2𝑊\sum_{j\in[m]}x_{j}=2W; and the question is to decide if there exists a subset of indices J⊆[m]𝐽delimited-[]𝑚J\subseteq[m] such that ∑j∈Jxj=Wsubscript𝑗𝐽subscript𝑥𝑗𝑊\sum_{j\in J}x_{j}=W. Given an instance (x1,x2,…,xm)subscript𝑥1subscript𝑥2…subscript𝑥𝑚(x_{1},x_{2},\ldots,x_{m}) of Partition, we construct an instance (G,N,𝒱)𝐺𝑁𝒱(G,N,\mathcal{V}) of [𝚇]delimited-[]𝚇[\mathtt{X}]-(α,β)𝛼𝛽({\alpha},{\beta})-Compact-FD as follows, (where [𝚇]delimited-[]𝚇[\mathtt{X}] is a placeholder for proportionality or envy-freeness and completeness or envy-freeness and Pareto-optimality). We take N𝑁N = [2]; and for every j∈[m]𝑗delimited-[]𝑚j\in[m], introduce an item zjsubscript𝑧𝑗z_{j}. We take G𝐺G to be the clique with vertex set {zj|j∈[m]}conditional-setsubscript𝑧𝑗𝑗delimited-[]𝑚\left\{{z_{j}~{}|~{}j\in[m]}\right\}. Finally, for i∈N𝑖𝑁i\in N, we define the valuation function vi:V​(G)→ℚ≥0:subscript𝑣𝑖→𝑉𝐺subscriptℚabsent0{{v_{i}}:{V(G)}\rightarrow{\mathbb{Q}_{\geq 0}}}, where vi​(zj)=xjsubscript𝑣𝑖subscript𝑧𝑗subscript𝑥𝑗v_{i}(z_{j})=x_{j}. Notice that for any Z⊆V​(G)𝑍𝑉𝐺Z\subseteq V(G), the subgraph G​[Z]𝐺delimited-[]𝑍G[Z] is a clique and hence (strongly) (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta)-compact. Now, an allocation π:N→2V​(G):𝜋→𝑁superscript2𝑉𝐺{{\pi}:{N}\rightarrow{2^{V(G)}}} is proportional (or complete and envy-free, or Pareto-optimal and envy-free) if and only if vi​(π​(i))=∑zj∈π​(i)xj=(1/2)⋅∑j=1mxj=Wsubscript𝑣𝑖𝜋𝑖subscriptsubscript𝑧𝑗𝜋𝑖subscript𝑥𝑗⋅12superscriptsubscript𝑗1𝑚subscript𝑥𝑗𝑊v_{i}(\pi(i))=\sum_{z_{j}\in\pi(i)}x_{j}=(1/2)\cdot\sum_{j=1}^{m}x_{j}=W for each i∈[2]𝑖delimited-[]2i\in[2].∎

We now consider the special case of [𝚇]delimited-[]𝚇[\mathtt{X}]-(α,β)𝛼𝛽({\alpha},{\beta})-Compact-FD when the maximum degree of the graph G𝐺G is bounded, where [𝚇]delimited-[]𝚇[\mathtt{X}] is a placeholder for proportionality, envy-freeness or maximin fairness. Consider (G,N,𝒱)𝐺𝑁𝒱(G,N,\mathcal{V}), where the degree of each vertex of G𝐺G is at most ΔΔ\Delta. Observe then that for every z∈V​(G)𝑧𝑉𝐺z\in V(G) and for every j≥0𝑗0j\geq 0, the number of vertices of G𝐺G that are at a distance of j𝑗j from z𝑧z is at most ΔjsuperscriptΔ𝑗\Delta^{j}. We thus have |BG​(z,β)|≤∑j=0βΔj≤Δβ+1subscript𝐵𝐺𝑧𝛽superscriptsubscript𝑗0𝛽superscriptΔ𝑗superscriptΔ𝛽1{|{B_{G}(z,\beta)}|}\leq\sum_{j=0}^{\beta}\Delta^{j}\leq\Delta^{\beta+1}. As an immediate consequence of this observation, we derive the following lemma.

To prove the lemma, we will bound the possible number of choices for an (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta)-compact allocation.Consider any (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta)-compact allocation π:N→2V​(G):𝜋→𝑁superscript2𝑉𝐺{{\pi}:{N}\rightarrow{2^{V(G)}}}. Now, the number of choices for (zi1,zi2,…,ziα)superscriptsubscript𝑧𝑖1superscriptsubscript𝑧𝑖2…superscriptsubscript𝑧𝑖𝛼(z_{i}^{1},z_{i}^{2},\ldots,z_{i}^{\alpha}) is at most mαsuperscript𝑚𝛼m^{\alpha}; and the number of choices for π​(i)𝜋𝑖\pi(i) such that π​(i)⊆⋃j=1αBG​(zij,β)𝜋𝑖superscriptsubscript𝑗1𝛼subscript𝐵𝐺superscriptsubscript𝑧𝑖𝑗𝛽\pi(i)\subseteq\bigcup_{j=1}^{\alpha}B_{G}(z_{i}^{j},\beta) is at most 2α⋅Δ𝒪​(β)superscript2⋅𝛼superscriptΔ𝒪𝛽2^{\alpha\cdot\Delta^{\mathcal{O}(\beta)}}. Notice that once we enumerate all (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta)-compact allocations, we can verify if at least one of those allocations is fair, with respect to the fairness concepts proportionality, envy-freeness and maximin-fairness. Thus, as an immediate consequence of Lemma 3.2, we have the following result.

We can use the same idea as in Lemma 3.2 to solve the strongly (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta)-compact variants of the problems as well. Consider an instance (G,N,𝒱)𝐺𝑁𝒱(G,N,\mathcal{V}) of [𝚇]delimited-[]𝚇[\mathtt{X}]-(1,0)10({1},{0})-Compact-FD. Observe that for any (1,0)10(1,0)-compact allocation π𝜋\pi, we have |π​(i)|≤1𝜋𝑖1{|{\pi(i)}|}\leq 1 for every i∈N𝑖𝑁i\in N. That is, every agent must receive at most one item. In this case, we can reduce the problems corresponding to proportionality and maximin fairness to matching problems. For proportionality, we construct the agents-items bipartite graph H𝐻H: there is a vertex in H𝐻H for every agent i∈N𝑖𝑁i\in N and there is a vertex in H𝐻H for every item z∈V​(G)𝑧𝑉𝐺z\in V(G). And there is an edge in H𝐻H between i∈N𝑖𝑁i\in N and z∈V​(G)𝑧𝑉𝐺z\in V(G) if vi​(z)≥(1/n)⋅vi​(V​(G))subscript𝑣𝑖𝑧⋅1𝑛subscript𝑣𝑖𝑉𝐺v_{i}(z)\geq(1/n)\cdot v_{i}(V(G)). Notice that (G,N,𝒱)𝐺𝑁𝒱(G,N,\mathcal{V}) admits an allocation that is proportional and (1,0)10(1,0)-compact if and only if H𝐻H has a matching that saturates all the agent-vertices, which we can check in polynomial time.

For maximin fairness, we apply an identical procedure. Recall that Γ​((1,0)−𝚌𝚘𝚖)Γ10𝚌𝚘𝚖\Gamma((1,0)\mathchar 45\relax\mathtt{com}) denotes the set of all (1,0)10(1,0) compact allocations. For convenience, in this section, we simply write 𝚖𝚖𝚜isubscript𝚖𝚖𝚜𝑖\mathtt{mms}_{i} for Γ​((1,0)−𝚌𝚘𝚖)−𝚖𝚖𝚜i​(G,N,𝒱)Γ10𝚌𝚘𝚖subscript𝚖𝚖𝚜𝑖𝐺𝑁𝒱\Gamma((1,0)\mathchar 45\relax\mathtt{com})\mathchar 45\relax\mathtt{mms}_{i}(G,N,\mathcal{V}) for every i∈N𝑖𝑁i\in N. As before, we construct the agents-items bipartite graph H𝐻H but with the following difference. There is an edge in H𝐻H between i𝑖i and z𝑧z if and only if vi​(z)≥𝚖𝚖𝚜isubscript𝑣𝑖𝑧subscript𝚖𝚖𝚜𝑖v_{i}(z)\geq\mathtt{mms}_{i}.The rest of the arguments are identical to that of proportionality. To construct H𝐻H, we need to compute 𝚖𝚖𝚜isubscript𝚖𝚖𝚜𝑖\mathtt{mms}_{i}. But this is quite straightforward, as 𝚖𝚖𝚜i=0subscript𝚖𝚖𝚜𝑖0\mathtt{mms}_{i}=0 if m<n𝑚𝑛m<n and otherwise, 𝚖𝚖𝚜isubscript𝚖𝚖𝚜𝑖\mathtt{mms}_{i} is precisely the value agent i𝑖i has for her n𝑛nth most valued item. We relegate a formal proof of this fact to the appendix.

{toappendix}Recall that Γ​((1,0)−𝚌𝚘𝚖)Γ10𝚌𝚘𝚖\Gamma((1,0)\mathchar 45\relax\mathtt{com}) denotes the set of all (1,0)10(1,0) compact allocations. For convenience, in this section, we simply write 𝚖𝚖𝚜isubscript𝚖𝚖𝚜𝑖\mathtt{mms}_{i} for Γ​((1,0)−𝚌𝚘𝚖)−𝚖𝚖𝚜i​(G,N,𝒱)Γ10𝚌𝚘𝚖subscript𝚖𝚖𝚜𝑖𝐺𝑁𝒱\Gamma((1,0)\mathchar 45\relax\mathtt{com})\mathchar 45\relax\mathtt{mms}_{i}(G,N,\mathcal{V}) for every i∈N𝑖𝑁i\in N. The following lemma says that 𝚖𝚖𝚜i=0subscript𝚖𝚖𝚜𝑖0\mathtt{mms}_{i}=0 if m<n𝑚𝑛m<n; and otherwise, 𝚖𝚖𝚜isubscript𝚖𝚖𝚜𝑖\mathtt{mms}_{i} is precisely the value agent i𝑖i has for her n𝑛nth most valued item.

Fix i∈[n]𝑖delimited-[]𝑛i\in[n]. Suppose first that m<n𝑚𝑛m<n. Then, for any (1,0)10(1,0)-compact allocation π′:[n]→2V​(G):superscript𝜋′→delimited-[]𝑛superscript2𝑉𝐺{{\pi^{\prime}}:{[n]}\rightarrow{2^{V(G)}}}, there exists an agent j′∈[n]superscript𝑗′delimited-[]𝑛j^{\prime}\in[n] such that π′​(j′)=∅superscript𝜋′superscript𝑗′\pi^{\prime}(j^{\prime})=\emptyset. Hence vi​(π′​(j′))=0subscript𝑣𝑖superscript𝜋′superscript𝑗′0v_{i}(\pi^{\prime}(j^{\prime}))=0, and in particular, minj∈N⁡vi​(π′​(j))=vi​(π′​(j′))=0subscript𝑗𝑁subscript𝑣𝑖superscript𝜋′𝑗subscript𝑣𝑖superscript𝜋′superscript𝑗′0\min_{j\in N}v_{i}(\pi^{\prime}(j))=v_{i}(\pi^{\prime}(j^{\prime}))=0. Thus, by the definition of 𝚖𝚖𝚜isubscript𝚖𝚖𝚜𝑖\mathtt{mms}_{i}, we have 𝚖𝚖𝚜i=0subscript𝚖𝚖𝚜𝑖0\mathtt{mms}_{i}=0. Suppose now that m≥n𝑚𝑛m\geq n. Consider the ordering σi=(z1i,z2i,…,zmi)superscript𝜎𝑖subscriptsuperscript𝑧𝑖1subscriptsuperscript𝑧𝑖2…subscriptsuperscript𝑧𝑖𝑚\sigma^{i}=(z^{i}_{1},z^{i}_{2},\ldots,z^{i}_{m}). Recall that we have vi​(z1i)≥vi​(z2i)≥⋯≥vi​(zmi)subscript𝑣𝑖subscriptsuperscript𝑧𝑖1subscript𝑣𝑖subscriptsuperscript𝑧𝑖2⋯subscript𝑣𝑖subscriptsuperscript𝑧𝑖𝑚v_{i}(z^{i}_{1})\geq v_{i}(z^{i}_{2})\geq\cdots\geq v_{i}(z^{i}_{m}). We will show that 𝚖𝚖𝚜i=vi​(zni)subscript𝚖𝚖𝚜𝑖subscript𝑣𝑖subscriptsuperscript𝑧𝑖𝑛\mathtt{mms}_{i}=v_{i}(z^{i}_{n}). Let us first see that 𝚖𝚖𝚜i≥vi​(zni)subscript𝚖𝚖𝚜𝑖subscript𝑣𝑖subscriptsuperscript𝑧𝑖𝑛\mathtt{mms}_{i}\geq v_{i}(z^{i}_{n}). Let us now see that 𝚖𝚖𝚜i≤vi​(zni)subscript𝚖𝚖𝚜𝑖subscript𝑣𝑖subscriptsuperscript𝑧𝑖𝑛\mathtt{mms}_{i}\leq v_{i}(z^{i}_{n}). So, assume that ϕ​(j)≠∅italic-ϕ𝑗\phi(j)\neq\emptyset for every j∈[n]𝑗delimited-[]𝑛j\in[n]. Since there are n𝑛n agents, and since ϕitalic-ϕ\phi is (1,0)10(1,0)-compact, ϕitalic-ϕ\phi allocates exactly n𝑛n items. In particular, ϕitalic-ϕ\phi allocates zrisubscriptsuperscript𝑧𝑖𝑟z^{i}_{r} for some r≥n𝑟𝑛r\geq n, which implies that 𝚖𝚖𝚜i=minj∈[n]⁡vi​(ϕ​(j))≤vi​(zri)≤vi​(zni)subscript𝚖𝚖𝚜𝑖subscript𝑗delimited-[]𝑛subscript𝑣𝑖italic-ϕ𝑗subscript𝑣𝑖subscriptsuperscript𝑧𝑖𝑟subscript𝑣𝑖subscriptsuperscript𝑧𝑖𝑛\mathtt{mms}_{i}=\min_{j\in[n]}v_{i}(\phi(j))\leq v_{i}(z^{i}_{r})\leq v_{i}(z^{i}_{n}). We have thus shown that 𝚖𝚖𝚜i=vi​(zni)subscript𝚖𝚖𝚜𝑖subscript𝑣𝑖subscriptsuperscript𝑧𝑖𝑛\mathtt{mms}_{i}=v_{i}(z^{i}_{n}).∎

As for envy-freeness, we impose the additional constraint that each agent must receive exactly one item. Then the corresponding problem is polynomial time solvable, as shown by Gan et al. (2019). Their algorithm is also a matching algorithm, which works as follows: We construct the graph where each agent is adjacent to her most valued items and look for a matching that saturates all agents or a Hall’s violator. If we find a matching, then we have an envy-free allocation; else, we remove a Hall’s violator and recurse.

We thus have the following result.

Observe that (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta)-compactness is not a subgraph-closed property. That is, if G𝐺G is an (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta)-compact graph and H𝐻H is a subgraph of G𝐺G then H𝐻H need not necessarily be (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta)-compact. But if H𝐻H is a connected component of G𝐺G, then H𝐻H necessarily is (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta)-compact. Moreover, the diameter of H𝐻H is bounded by 𝒪​(α​β)𝒪𝛼𝛽\mathcal{O}(\alpha\beta). We record this fact below.

Since G𝐺G is (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta)-compact, there exist vertices z1,z2,…,zα∈V​(G)subscript𝑧1subscript𝑧2…subscript𝑧𝛼𝑉𝐺z_{1},z_{2},\ldots,z_{\alpha}\in V(G) such that V​(G)=⋃j∈[α]BG​(zj,β)𝑉𝐺subscript𝑗delimited-[]𝛼subscript𝐵𝐺subscript𝑧𝑗𝛽V(G)=\bigcup_{j\in[\alpha]}B_{G}(z_{j},\beta). Consider a connected component H𝐻H of G𝐺G. Notice that for every j∈[α]𝑗delimited-[]𝛼j\in[\alpha] such that H𝐻H contains the vertex zjsubscript𝑧𝑗z_{j}, we have BG​(zj,β)=BH​(zj,β)subscript𝐵𝐺subscript𝑧𝑗𝛽subscript𝐵𝐻subscript𝑧𝑗𝛽B_{G}(z_{j},\beta)=B_{H}(z_{j},\beta). Let J={j∈[α]|zj∈V​(H)}𝐽conditional-set𝑗delimited-[]𝛼subscript𝑧𝑗𝑉𝐻J=\left\{{j\in[\alpha]~{}|~{}z_{j}\in V(H)}\right\}. Then V​(H)=⋃j∈JBH​(zj,β)𝑉𝐻subscript𝑗𝐽subscript𝐵𝐻subscript𝑧𝑗𝛽V(H)=\bigcup_{j\in J}B_{H}(z_{j},\beta). As |J|≤α𝐽𝛼{|{J}|}\leq\alpha, H𝐻H is (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta)-compact.

To see that diam​(H)=𝒪​(α​β)diam𝐻𝒪𝛼𝛽\text{diam}(H)=\mathcal{O}(\alpha\beta), observe first that for every j∈J𝑗𝐽j\in J and z,z′∈BH​(zj,β)𝑧superscript𝑧′subscript𝐵𝐻subscript𝑧𝑗𝛽z,z^{\prime}\in B_{H}(z_{j},\beta), we have distH​(z,z′)≤distH​(z,zj)+distH​(zj,z′)≤2​βsubscriptdist𝐻𝑧superscript𝑧′subscriptdist𝐻𝑧subscript𝑧𝑗subscriptdist𝐻subscript𝑧𝑗superscript𝑧′2𝛽\text{dist}_{H}(z,z^{\prime})\leq\text{dist}_{H}(z,z_{j})+\text{dist}_{H}(z_{j},z^{\prime})\leq 2\beta. Now, consider an auxiliary graph H′superscript𝐻′H^{\prime} on |J|𝐽{|{J}|} vertices, defined as follows. For each j∈J𝑗𝐽j\in J, H′superscript𝐻′H^{\prime} contains a vertex yjsubscript𝑦𝑗y_{j}. For j,j′∈J𝑗superscript𝑗′𝐽j,j^{\prime}\in J, yj​yj′∈E​(H′)subscript𝑦𝑗subscript𝑦superscript𝑗′𝐸superscript𝐻′y_{j}y_{j^{\prime}}\in E(H^{\prime}) if and only if either BH​(zj,β)∩BH​(zj′,β)≠∅subscript𝐵𝐻subscript𝑧𝑗𝛽subscript𝐵𝐻subscript𝑧superscript𝑗′𝛽B_{H}(z_{j},\beta)\cap B_{H}(z_{j^{\prime}},\beta)\neq\emptyset or there exists z∈BH​(zj,β)𝑧subscript𝐵𝐻subscript𝑧𝑗𝛽z\in B_{H}(z_{j},\beta) and z′∈BH​(zj′,β)superscript𝑧′subscript𝐵𝐻subscript𝑧superscript𝑗′𝛽z^{\prime}\in B_{H}(z_{j^{\prime}},\beta) such that z​z′∈E​(H)𝑧superscript𝑧′𝐸𝐻zz^{\prime}\in E(H). (Informally, H′superscript𝐻′H^{\prime} is obtained from H𝐻H by “contracting” each BH​(zj,β)subscript𝐵𝐻subscript𝑧𝑗𝛽B_{H}(z_{j},\beta) into a single vertex). Observe that we can “expand out” each vertex of Q𝑄Q to a path in H𝐻H of length at most 2​β2𝛽2\beta; and thus obtain a z𝑧z-z′superscript𝑧′z^{\prime} path of length 𝒪​(α​β)𝒪𝛼𝛽\mathcal{O}(\alpha\beta). Hence distH​(z,z′)=𝒪​(α​β)subscriptdist𝐻𝑧superscript𝑧′𝒪𝛼𝛽\text{dist}_{H}(z,z^{\prime})=\mathcal{O}(\alpha\beta) for every z,z′∈V​(H)𝑧superscript𝑧′𝑉𝐻z,z^{\prime}\in V(H), and the lemma follows.∎

Consider a graph G𝐺G. Suppose that G𝐺G is a (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta)-compact. Consider a graph G𝐺G, a set of agents N=[n]𝑁delimited-[]𝑛N=[n] and an ordered sequence (z^i)i∈Nsubscriptsubscript^𝑧𝑖𝑖𝑁(\hat{z}_{i})_{i\in N} of n𝑛n distinct vertices z^i∈V​(G)subscript^𝑧𝑖𝑉𝐺\hat{z}_{i}\in V(G). Notice that πτsuperscript𝜋𝜏\pi^{\tau} is a ((z^i)i∈[n];β+1)subscriptsubscript^𝑧𝑖𝑖delimited-[]𝑛𝛽1((\hat{z}_{i})_{i\in[n]};\beta+1)-annotated allocation. Conversely, suppose there exists τ=(Ci)i∈[n]∈T𝜏subscriptsubscript𝐶𝑖𝑖delimited-[]𝑛𝑇\tau=(C_{i})_{i\in[n]}\in T such that ϕ:[n]→2V​(Gτ):italic-ϕ→delimited-[]𝑛superscript2𝑉superscript𝐺𝜏{{\phi}:{[n]}\rightarrow{2^{V(G^{\tau})}}} is a ((z^i)i∈[n];β+1)subscriptsubscript^𝑧𝑖𝑖delimited-[]𝑛𝛽1((\hat{z}_{i})_{i\in[n]};\beta+1)-annotated allocation. Then the allocation ϕ′:[n]→2V​(G):superscriptitalic-ϕ′→delimited-[]𝑛superscript2𝑉𝐺{{\phi^{\prime}}:{[n]}\rightarrow{2^{V(G)}}} defined by ϕ′​(i)=ϕ​(i)∖{z^i}superscriptitalic-ϕ′𝑖italic-ϕ𝑖subscript^𝑧𝑖\phi^{\prime}(i)=\phi(i)\setminus\left\{{\hat{z}_{i}}\right\} for every i∈[n]𝑖delimited-[]𝑛i\in[n] is an (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta)-compact allocation. Also, as viτ​(ϕ​(j))=vi​(ϕ′​(j))subscriptsuperscript𝑣𝜏𝑖italic-ϕ𝑗subscript𝑣𝑖superscriptitalic-ϕ′𝑗v^{\tau}_{i}(\phi(j))=v_{i}(\phi^{\prime}(j)), ϕitalic-ϕ\phi is [𝚇]delimited-[]𝚇[\mathtt{X}]-fair if and only if ϕ′superscriptitalic-ϕ′\phi^{\prime} is [𝚇]delimited-[]𝚇[\mathtt{X}]-fair.∎

Consider the recognition problem for (strongly) (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta)-compact graphs. That is, the problem of testing whether a given a graph G𝐺G is (strongly) (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta)-compact (for fixed α𝛼\alpha and β𝛽\beta). We can recognise (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta)-compact graphs in polynomial time. Given an m𝑚m-vertex graph G𝐺G, for every choice of α𝛼\alpha vertices z1,z2,…,zαsubscript𝑧1subscript𝑧2…subscript𝑧𝛼z_{1},z_{2},\ldots,z_{\alpha}, we can check if V​(G)=⋃j∈[α]BG​(zj,β)𝑉𝐺subscript𝑗delimited-[]𝛼subscript𝐵𝐺subscript𝑧𝑗𝛽V(G)=\bigcup_{j\in[\alpha]}B_{G}(z_{j},\beta), and we can do this in time m𝒪​(1)superscript𝑚𝒪1m^{\mathcal{O}(1)}. Since the number of choices for z1,z2,…,zαsubscript𝑧1subscript𝑧2…subscript𝑧𝛼z_{1},z_{2},\ldots,z_{\alpha} is m𝒪​(α)superscript𝑚𝒪𝛼m^{\mathcal{O}(\alpha)}, we conclude that we can check if G𝐺G is (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta)-compact in time m𝒪​(α)superscript𝑚𝒪𝛼m^{\mathcal{O}(\alpha)}. As α𝛼\alpha is a constant, we can indeed check if G𝐺G is (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta)-compact in polynomial time. For future reference, we note this fact.

But the case of strong compactness is quite different. For α≤2𝛼2\alpha\leq 2, the problem is polynomial time solvable and for α≥3𝛼3\alpha\geq 3, the problem is NP-hard. If α=1𝛼1\alpha=1, then the problem is the same as testing whether diam​(G)≤βdiam𝐺𝛽\text{diam}(G)\leq\beta, which can be done in polynomial time. If α=2𝛼2\alpha=2, then the problem can be reduced to 2-SAT, and hence it can still be solved in polynomial time. For α≥3𝛼3\alpha\geq 3, the problem is NP-hard even when β=1𝛽1\beta=1. Notice that G𝐺G is strongly (α,1)𝛼1(\alpha,1)-compact if and only if there exist V1,V2​…,Vα⊆V​(G)subscript𝑉1subscript𝑉2…subscript𝑉𝛼𝑉𝐺V_{1},V_{2}\ldots,V_{\alpha}\subseteq V(G) such that G​[Vi]𝐺delimited-[]subscript𝑉𝑖G[V_{i}] is a clique for every i∈[α]𝑖delimited-[]𝛼i\in[\alpha]. So checking whether G𝐺G is (α,1)𝛼1(\alpha,1)-compact is the same as checking whether V​(G)𝑉𝐺V(G) can be covered by α𝛼\alpha cliques. This problem is called the α𝛼\alpha-Clique Cover problem, which is known to be NP-complete (Karp, 1972). In fact, notice that α𝛼\alpha-Clique Cover is equivalent to the well known α𝛼\alpha-Colouring problem. For a graph G𝐺G, V​(G)𝑉𝐺V(G) can be covered using α𝛼\alpha cliques if and only if V​(G¯)𝑉¯𝐺V(\overline{G}) can be covered using α𝛼\alpha independent sets, where G¯¯𝐺\overline{G} is the complement of G𝐺G; and V​(G¯)𝑉¯𝐺V(\overline{G}) can be covered using α𝛼\alpha independent sets if and only if the vertices of G¯¯𝐺\overline{G} can be properly coloured using α𝛼\alpha colours.

In this section, we establish the strong NP-hardness of (strongly) compact fair division.We consider three fairness and efficiency combinations (i) proportionality, (ii) envy-freeness plus completeness and (iii) envy-freeness plus Pareto-optimality. For each of these three choices,we show that the corresponding problem of checking whether there exists a fair (strongly) (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta)-compact allocation is strongly NP-hard for every fixed α≥3𝛼3\alpha\geq 3 and β≥0𝛽0\beta\geq 0.555Notice that even though Theorem 3.1 already establishes the NP-hardness of these problems for α,β≥1𝛼𝛽1\alpha,\beta\geq 1, Theorem 3.1 only implies weak NP-hardness, as we reduced from Partition. Besides Theorem 3.1 only works for β≥1𝛽1\beta\geq 1.These results hold even the graph G𝐺G is edgeless and thus has treewidth 00. And notice that if G𝐺G is an edgeless graph, then for any S⊆V​(G)𝑆𝑉𝐺S\subseteq V(G), G​[S]𝐺delimited-[]𝑆G[S] is (strongly) (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta)-compact if and only if |S|≤α𝑆𝛼{|{S}|}\leq\alpha.For proportionality and envy-freeness + completeness, we also show that the strongly compact variants of the corresponding problems are NP-hard for every fixed α,β≥1𝛼𝛽1\alpha,\beta\geq 1. All the hardness results hold even when the valuations are additive and polynomially bounded. We prove these results one by one.

Fix α≥3𝛼3\alpha\geq 3 and β≥0𝛽0\beta\geq 0. Membership of Prop-(α,β)𝛼𝛽({\alpha},{\beta})-Compact-FD in NP is trivial: Given an allocation, we can verify in polynomial time whether it is proportional, and by Remark 3.11, we can also verify whether the allocation is (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta)-compact. We now show NP-hardness by a reduction from Exact Cover by α𝛼{\alpha}-Sets (Xα𝛼\alphaC), which is known to be NP-complete for every α≥3𝛼3\alpha\geq 3 (Karp, 1972).666Exact Cover by 33{3}-Sets (X333C) was, in fact, one of Karp’s original 21 NP-complete problems (Karp, 1972). The original paper of Karp only shows the NP hardness of X3C. But it is straightforward to extend that result to show NP-hardness of Xα𝛼\alphaC for every α>3𝛼3\alpha>3 as well. For the sake of completeness, we sketch a proof of this fact in the appendix. In Xα𝛼\alphaC, the input consists of a set X𝑋X such that |X|=α​s𝑋𝛼𝑠{|{X}|}=\alpha s and a family ℱ⊆2Xℱsuperscript2𝑋\mathcal{F}\subseteq 2^{X} of subsets of X𝑋X such that |F|=α𝐹𝛼{|{F}|}=\alpha for every F∈ℱ𝐹ℱF\in\mathcal{F}; and the question is to decide if (X,ℱ)𝑋ℱ(X,\mathcal{F}) has an exact cover, i.e., a sub-family ℱ′⊆ℱsuperscriptℱ′ℱ\mathcal{F}^{\prime}\subseteq\mathcal{F} such that |ℱ′|=ssuperscriptℱ′𝑠{|{\mathcal{F}^{\prime}}|}=s and ⋃F∈ℱ′F=Xsubscript𝐹superscriptℱ′𝐹𝑋\bigcup_{F\in\mathcal{F}^{\prime}}F=X. Consider an instance (X,ℱ)𝑋ℱ(X,\mathcal{F}) of Xα𝛼\alphaC.Let |F|=r𝐹𝑟{|{F}|}=r and ℱ={F1,F2,…,Fr}ℱsubscript𝐹1subscript𝐹2…subscript𝐹𝑟\mathcal{F}=\left\{{F_{1},F_{2},\ldots,F_{r}}\right\}. In this case, we return a trivial yes-instance or no-instance of Prop-(α,β)𝛼𝛽({\alpha},{\beta})-Compact-FD accordingly. So from now on, we assume without loss of generality that r>s𝑟𝑠r>s. As ϕitalic-ϕ\phi is (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta)-compact and G𝐺G is edge-less, we have |ϕ​(i)|≤αitalic-ϕ𝑖𝛼{|{\phi(i)}|}\leq\alpha, and hence ϕ​(i)={wx|x∈Fi}italic-ϕ𝑖conditional-setsubscript𝑤𝑥𝑥subscript𝐹𝑖\phi(i)=\left\{{w_{x}~{}|~{}x\in F_{i}}\right\} for every i∈R𝑖𝑅i\in R. Also, since ϕitalic-ϕ\phi is an allocation, for distinct i,i′∈R𝑖superscript𝑖′𝑅i,i^{\prime}\in R, we have ϕ​(i)∩ϕ​(i′)=∅italic-ϕ𝑖italic-ϕsuperscript𝑖′\phi(i)\cap\phi(i^{\prime})=\emptyset and hence Fi∩Fi′=∅subscript𝐹𝑖subscript𝐹superscript𝑖′F_{i}\cap F_{i^{\prime}}=\emptyset. By slightly modifying the reduction in the proof of Theorem 4.1, we can show that Complete-EF-(α,β)𝛼𝛽({\alpha},{\beta})-Compact-FD is NP-complete as well. We only sketch the proof below as the arguments are identical to the ones used to prove Theorem 4.1.

Membership in NP is again trivial. To show hardness, we reduce from (Xα𝛼\alphaC). Given an instance (X,ℱ)𝑋ℱ(X,\mathcal{F}), we proceed exactly as in the proof of Theorem 4.1 except for the following three differences. (1) We no longer have the agent r+1𝑟1r+1. That is, N=[r]𝑁delimited-[]𝑟N=[r] and i∈N𝑖𝑁i\in N corresponds to the set Fi∈ℱsubscript𝐹𝑖ℱF_{i}\in\mathcal{F}. (2) We no longer have the item y∗subscript𝑦y_{*}. Thus the graph G𝐺G is the edge-less graph with vertex set V​(G)={yj|j∈[r−s]}∪{wx|x∈X}𝑉𝐺conditional-setsubscript𝑦𝑗𝑗delimited-[]𝑟𝑠conditional-setsubscript𝑤𝑥𝑥𝑋V(G)=\left\{{y_{j}~{}|~{}j\in[r-s]}\right\}\cup\left\{{w_{x}~{}|~{}x\in X}\right\}. (3) We modify the valuation functions to ensure that (a) vi​({wx|x∈X})=vi​({yj})subscript𝑣𝑖conditional-setsubscript𝑤𝑥𝑥𝑋subscript𝑣𝑖subscript𝑦𝑗v_{i}(\{w_{x}~{}|~{}x\in X\})=v_{i}(\{y_{j}\}) for every i∈[r]𝑖delimited-[]𝑟i\in[r] and j∈[r−s]𝑗delimited-[]𝑟𝑠j\in[r-s] and (b) ∑z∈V​(G)vi​(z)=1subscript𝑧𝑉𝐺subscript𝑣𝑖𝑧1\sum_{z\in V(G)}v_{i}(z)=1 for every i∈N𝑖𝑁i\in N. Specifically, we define the valuation functions as follows. Consider i∈[r]𝑖delimited-[]𝑟i\in[r]. For x∈X𝑥𝑋x\in X, we havevi​(wx)={1/(α​r−α​s+α), if ​x∈Fi0, if ​x∉Fi;subscript𝑣𝑖subscript𝑤𝑥cases1𝛼𝑟𝛼𝑠𝛼 if 𝑥subscript𝐹𝑖0 if 𝑥subscript𝐹𝑖v_{i}(w_{x})=\begin{cases}1/(\alpha r-\alpha s+\alpha),&\text{ if }x\in F_{i}\\0,&\text{ if }x\notin F_{i};\end{cases}and vi​(yj)=1/(r−s+1)subscript𝑣𝑖subscript𝑦𝑗1𝑟𝑠1v_{i}(y_{j})=1/(r-s+1) for every j∈[r−s]𝑗delimited-[]𝑟𝑠j\in[r-s].

Now, if (X,ℱ)𝑋ℱ(X,\mathcal{F}) is a yes-instance, then we have an exact cover ℱ′⊆ℱsuperscriptℱ′ℱ\mathcal{F}^{\prime}\subseteq\mathcal{F} for (X,ℱ)𝑋ℱ(X,\mathcal{F}); and we define an allocation π:N→2V​(G):𝜋→𝑁superscript2𝑉𝐺{{\pi}:{N}\rightarrow{2^{V(G)}}} exactly as in the proof of Theorem 4.1. Conversely, consider a complete, envy-free, (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta)-compact allocation ϕ:N→2V​(G):italic-ϕ→𝑁superscript2𝑉𝐺{{\phi}:{N}\rightarrow{2^{V(G)}}}. And using exactly the same arguments that we used in Theorem 4.1, we can prove that |R|=s𝑅𝑠{|{R}|}=s and {Fi|i∈R}conditional-setsubscript𝐹𝑖𝑖𝑅\left\{{F_{i}~{}|~{}i\in R}\right\} is an exact cover for (X,ℱ)𝑋ℱ(X,\mathcal{F}).∎

In Theorem 4.2, we can replace completeness with Pareto-optimality, and the reduction would still hold. We thus have the following result.

We use the same reduction as in the proof of Theorem 4.2, and construct the instance (G,N,𝒱)𝐺𝑁𝒱(G,N,\mathcal{V}) of PO-EF-(α,β)𝛼𝛽({\alpha},{\beta})-Compact-FD from a given instance (X,ℱ)𝑋ℱ(X,\mathcal{F}) of Xα𝛼\alphaC.

Notice that the allocation π𝜋\pi that we defined in the forward direction of the proof of Theorem 4.2 is indeed Pareto-optimal. To prove this, it is sufficient to prove that π𝜋\pi achieves the maximum utilitarian social welfare, i.e., ∑i∈Nvi​(π​(i))=maxπ′​∑i∈Nvi​(π′​(i))subscript𝑖𝑁subscript𝑣𝑖𝜋𝑖subscriptsuperscript𝜋′subscript𝑖𝑁subscript𝑣𝑖superscript𝜋′𝑖\sum_{i\in N}v_{i}(\pi(i))=\max_{\pi^{\prime}}\sum_{i\in N}{v_{i}(\pi^{\prime}(i))}, where the maximum is over all allocations π′:N→2V​(G):superscript𝜋′→𝑁superscript2𝑉𝐺{{\pi^{\prime}}:{N}\rightarrow{2^{V(G)}}}. Notice that for each z∈V​(G)𝑧𝑉𝐺z\in V(G), if π𝜋\pi allocates z𝑧z to agent i𝑖i, then for every agent i′superscript𝑖′i^{\prime}, we have vi​(z)≥vi′​(z)subscript𝑣𝑖𝑧subscript𝑣superscript𝑖′𝑧v_{i}(z)\geq v_{i^{\prime}}(z). This fact, along with the completeness of π𝜋\pi and the additivity of visubscript𝑣𝑖v_{i}s, implies that π𝜋\pi achieves the maximum utilitarian social welfare. To prove this formally, we can first verify that ∑i∈Nvi​(π​(i))=(α​s)/(α​r−α​s+1)+(r−s)/(r−s+1)=r/(r−s+1)subscript𝑖𝑁subscript𝑣𝑖𝜋𝑖𝛼𝑠𝛼𝑟𝛼𝑠1𝑟𝑠𝑟𝑠1𝑟𝑟𝑠1\sum_{i\in N}v_{i}(\pi(i))=(\alpha s)/(\alpha r-\alpha s+1)+(r-s)/(r-s+1)=r/(r-s+1).

Now, for any allocation π′:N→2V​(G):superscript𝜋′→𝑁superscript2𝑉𝐺{{\pi^{\prime}}:{N}\rightarrow{2^{V(G)}}}, we have,∑i∈Nvi​(π′​(i))subscript𝑖𝑁subscript𝑣𝑖superscript𝜋′𝑖\displaystyle\sum_{i\in N}v_{i}(\pi^{\prime}(i))≤∑z∈V​(G)maxi∈N⁡vi​(z)absentsubscript𝑧𝑉𝐺subscript𝑖𝑁subscript𝑣𝑖𝑧\displaystyle\leq\sum_{z\in V(G)}\max_{i\in N}v_{i}(z)=∑x∈Xmaxi∈N⁡vi​(wx)+∑j∈[r−s]maxi∈N⁡vi​(yj)absentsubscript𝑥𝑋subscript𝑖𝑁subscript𝑣𝑖subscript𝑤𝑥subscript𝑗delimited-[]𝑟𝑠subscript𝑖𝑁subscript𝑣𝑖subscript𝑦𝑗\displaystyle=\sum_{x\in X}\max_{i\in N}v_{i}(w_{x})+\sum_{j\in[r-s]}\max_{i\in N}v_{i}(y_{j})=∑x∈X1α​r−α​s+α+∑j∈[r−s]1r−s+1absentsubscript𝑥𝑋1𝛼𝑟𝛼𝑠𝛼subscript𝑗delimited-[]𝑟𝑠1𝑟𝑠1\displaystyle=\sum_{x\in X}\frac{1}{\alpha r-\alpha s+\alpha}+\sum_{j\in[r-s]}\frac{1}{r-s+1}=α​sα​r−α​s+α+r−sr−s+1absent𝛼𝑠𝛼𝑟𝛼𝑠𝛼𝑟𝑠𝑟𝑠1\displaystyle=\frac{\alpha s}{\alpha r-\alpha s+\alpha}+\frac{r-s}{r-s+1}=rr−s+1absent𝑟𝑟𝑠1\displaystyle=\frac{r}{r-s+1}=∑i∈Nvi​(π​(i)),absentsubscript𝑖𝑁subscript𝑣𝑖𝜋𝑖\displaystyle=\sum_{i\in N}v_{i}(\pi(i)),which shows that π𝜋\pi is Pareto-optimal.

For the backward direction, consider a Pareto-optimal, envy-free, (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta)-compact allocation ϕ:N→2V​(G):italic-ϕ→𝑁superscript2𝑉𝐺{{\phi}:{N}\rightarrow{2^{V(G)}}}. (Notice that in the proof of Theorem 4.2, we used completeness only to ensure that yjsubscript𝑦𝑗y_{j} is allocated (for at least one j∈[r−s]𝑗delimited-[]𝑟𝑠j\in[r-s]) under the allocation ϕitalic-ϕ\phi. And for this, Pareto-optimality is sufficient.) Every Pareto-optimal allocation must necessarily be complete, because for every z∈V​(G)𝑧𝑉𝐺z\in V(G), we have vi​(z)>0subscript𝑣𝑖𝑧0v_{i}(z)>0 for some i∈[r]𝑖delimited-[]𝑟i\in[r].777We can, in fact, argue that every Pareto-optimal, (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta)-compact allocation ϕitalic-ϕ\phi must necessarily allocate yjsubscript𝑦𝑗y_{j} for some j∈[r]𝑗delimited-[]𝑟j\in[r]. Suppose ϕitalic-ϕ\phi does not allocate yjsubscript𝑦𝑗y_{j} for every j∈[r−s]𝑗delimited-[]𝑟𝑠j\in[r-s]. Then ϕ([r])⊆{wx|x∈X}}\phi([r])\subseteq\{w_{x}~{}|~{}x\in X\}\}, which implies that |ϕ​([r])|≤α​sitalic-ϕdelimited-[]𝑟𝛼𝑠{|{\phi([r])}|}\leq\alpha s. Recall that we are under the assumption that r>s𝑟𝑠r>s. Since |ϕ​(i)|≤αitalic-ϕ𝑖𝛼{|{\phi(i)}|}\leq\alpha for every i∈[r]𝑖delimited-[]𝑟i\in[r], there exists an agent p∈[r]𝑝delimited-[]𝑟p\in[r] such that |ϕ​(p)|<αitalic-ϕ𝑝𝛼{|{\phi(p)}|}<\alpha. We can thus allocate y1subscript𝑦1y_{1} to p𝑝p, (while still ensuring (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta)-compactness), which will increase p𝑝p’s utility by vp​(y1)=1/(r−s+1)subscript𝑣𝑝subscript𝑦11𝑟𝑠1v_{p}(y_{1})=1/(r-s+1). And this contradicts the assumption that ϕitalic-ϕ\phi is Pareto-optimal.The rest of the arguments are identical to that in the proof of Theorem 4.2.∎

Observe that in each of the reductions in the proofs of Theorems 4.1, 4.2 and 4.3, the graph G𝐺G that we constructed is edge-less. Thus for S⊆V​(G)𝑆𝑉𝐺S\subseteq V(G), the subgraph G​[S]𝐺delimited-[]𝑆G[S] is (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta)-compact if and only if G​[S]𝐺delimited-[]𝑆G[S] is strongly (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta)-compact. Therefore, even if we replace compactness with strong compactness in Theorems  4.1, 4.2 and 4.3, the hardness results would still hold. We thus have the following result.

Theorems 4.1-4.4, however, do not cover the case when α≤2𝛼2\alpha\leq 2. We provide a partial answer for this case in Section 4.2. In particular, for the strongly compact variants, we show that the problems of proportional as well as envy-free + complete allocations are still NP-hardfor every α≥1𝛼1\alpha\geq 1 and β≥1𝛽1\beta\geq 1.

{toappendix}

Membership in NP is trivial. When α=3𝛼3\alpha=3, the problem is known to be NP-complete (Karp, 1972). Fix α>3𝛼3\alpha>3.To prove NP-hardness, we reduce from X3C. Fix α,β≥1𝛼𝛽1\alpha,\beta\geq 1.We show NP-hardness by a reduction from the β𝛽\beta-Club problem, in which the input consists of a graph H𝐻H and a non-negative integer k𝑘k, and the question is to decide if H𝐻H has a β𝛽\beta-club of size exactly k𝑘k. A β𝛽\beta-club in H𝐻H is a set of vertices S⊆V​(H)𝑆𝑉𝐻S\subseteq V(H) such that diam​(H​[S])≤βdiam𝐻delimited-[]𝑆𝛽\text{diam}(H[S])\leq\beta. So the β𝛽\beta-Club problem asks if H𝐻H contains a set of exactly k𝑘k vertices that induces a subgraph of diameter at most k𝑘k. This problem is NP-complete (Bourjolly et al., 2002).888We must note that there are two variants of the β𝛽\beta-Club problem: the “exact” and the “at least” variants. In the former, the task is to check if H𝐻H contains a β𝛽\beta-club of size exactly k𝑘k, whereas in the latter variant, the task is to check if H𝐻H contains a β𝛽\beta-club of size at least k𝑘k. Notice that these two problems are not equivalent. A graph H𝐻H may contain a β𝛽\beta-club of size k+1𝑘1k+1, yet no β𝛽\beta-club of size exactly k𝑘k. For example, take H𝐻H to be a cycle on 2​β+12𝛽12\beta+1 vertices and k=2​β𝑘2𝛽k=2\beta. Bourjolly et al. (2002) shows that the “at least” variant is NP-hard. But their reduction, in fact, proves the NP-hardness of the “exact” variant as well.

Consider an instance (H,k)𝐻𝑘(H,k) of β𝛽\beta-Club. Let V​(H)={z1,z2,…,zs}𝑉𝐻subscript𝑧1subscript𝑧2…subscript𝑧𝑠V(H)=\left\{{z_{1},z_{2},\ldots,z_{s}}\right\}. ∎

In this section, we consider we consider Prop-(1,β)1𝛽({1},{\beta})-Compact-FD and Prop-Str-(1,β)1𝛽({1},{\beta})-Compact-FD in a highly restricted input setting: The graph G𝐺G on the set of items is a path.We show that these problems admit FPT algorithms when parameterized by the number of agents, and XP algorithms when parameterized by the number of types of agents. We summarise the discussion so far in this section in the following theorem.

Now, notice that if the agents’ valuations are identical, then we need not keep track of the set N′superscript𝑁′N^{\prime} of agents who have already received a proportional and compact bundle of items. We simply need to remember how many agents have received bundles so far. That is, instead of going over all tuples (i,j,N′)𝑖𝑗superscript𝑁′(i,j,N^{\prime}), we only need to consider tuples (i,j,n′)𝑖𝑗superscript𝑛′(i,j,n^{\prime}), where 0≤n′≤n0superscript𝑛′𝑛0\leq n^{\prime}\leq n. And we can define and compute Aco​[i,j,n′]subscript𝐴co𝑖𝑗superscript𝑛′A_{\texttt{co}}[i,j,n^{\prime}] and Asco​[i,j,n′]subscript𝐴sco𝑖𝑗superscript𝑛′A_{\texttt{sco}}[i,j,n^{\prime}] accordingly. Thus instead of exponential dependence on the number of agents, the algorithm now will only have a polynomial dependence on the number of agents.

We can generalise the above idea further to design an XP algorithm parameterized by the number of types of agents. We say that two agents a,a′∈N𝑎superscript𝑎′𝑁a,a^{\prime}\in N are of the same type if their valuations are identical. Let p𝑝p be the number of distinct types of agents, and let the types be numbered from 111 to p𝑝p. Also for each q∈[p]𝑞delimited-[]𝑝q\in[p], let nqsubscript𝑛𝑞n_{q} be the number of agents of type q𝑞q and let vqsubscript𝑣𝑞v_{q} denote the valuation function corresponding to an agent of type j𝑗j. To design our algorithm, as before, we process the graph from z1subscript𝑧1z_{1} to zmsubscript𝑧𝑚z_{m}, and at every stage and for each type q∈[p]𝑞delimited-[]𝑝q\in[p], we keep track of the number of agents of type q𝑞q who received bundles. Again, we can solve the strongly compact variant by slightly modifying the DP. We thus have the following result.

In this section, we show that for each of the three fairness concepts—proportionality, envy-freeness and maximin fairness—the corresponding problem of checking if there exists an allocation that is fair and (α,β)𝛼𝛽(\alpha,\beta) compact admits a pseudo-XP algorithm, when parameterized by the treewidth of the item graph and the number of agents. We design a single dynamic programming procedure that works for all three fairness concepts. We assume in this section that the valuations are integer-valued. Throughout this section, we will use terminology and results from Sections 2, 3.4 and 3.5. We encourage the reader to have a quick look at these sections before reading any further.The following theorem is the main contribution of this section.

As an immediate corollary of Theorem 6.1, we derive the following result.

We now prove Theorem 6.1. In light of Lemma 3.9, to solve [𝚇]delimited-[]𝚇[\mathtt{X}]-(α,β)𝛼𝛽({\alpha},{\beta})-Compact-FD, it is enough to solve [𝚇]delimited-[]𝚇[\mathtt{X}]-Annotated-FD, for which we design an algorithm.More precisely, we prove the following lemma.

The rest of this section is dedicated to proving Lemma 6.3. And for that, we design a dynamic programming algorithm over a (nice) tree decomposition of G𝐺G.From now on, we assume that we are given (G,[n],𝒱,β,(z^i)i∈[n])𝐺delimited-[]𝑛𝒱𝛽subscriptsubscript^𝑧𝑖𝑖delimited-[]𝑛(G,[n],\mathcal{V},\beta,(\hat{z}_{i})_{i\in[n]}), an n2superscript𝑛2n^{2} tuple (w^i​j)i,j∈[n]subscriptsubscript^𝑤𝑖𝑗𝑖𝑗delimited-[]𝑛(\hat{w}_{ij})_{i,j\in[n]}, where 0≤wi​j≤W0subscript𝑤𝑖𝑗𝑊0\leq w_{ij}\leq W for every i,j∈[n]𝑖𝑗delimited-[]𝑛i,j\in[n] and a nice tree decomposition (T,{Xt|t∈V​(T)})𝑇conditional-setsubscript𝑋𝑡𝑡𝑉𝑇(T,\{X_{t}~{}|~{}t\in V(T)\}) of G𝐺G of width 𝗍𝗐𝗍𝗐{\sf tw}. We first add z^isubscriptbold-^𝑧𝑖\bm{\hat{z}_{i}} for every i∈[n]𝑖delimited-[]𝑛\bm{i\in[n]}, to every bag Xtsubscript𝑋𝑡\bm{X_{t}} (and denote the resulting tree decomposition by (T,{Xt|t∈V​(T)})𝑇conditional-setsubscript𝑋𝑡𝑡𝑉𝑇(T,\{X_{t}~{}|~{}t\in V(T)\}) as well). To design our DP, for each node t∈V​(T)𝑡𝑉𝑇t\in V(T), we first define a set 𝒞tsubscript𝒞𝑡\mathcal{C}_{t}, (which formalises our guesses). We now define what we call valid allocations. Informally, these are allocations that agree with our guesses (i.e., the tuples in 𝒞tsubscript𝒞𝑡\mathcal{C}_{t}). For a node t∈V​(T)𝑡𝑉𝑇t\in V(T), recall that Gtsubscript𝐺𝑡G_{t} is the subgraph of G𝐺G made up of all the vertices and edges introduced in the subtree rooted at t𝑡t.Consider a node t∈V​(T)𝑡𝑉𝑇t\in V(T) and an allocation π:[n]→2V​(Gt):𝜋→delimited-[]𝑛superscript2𝑉subscript𝐺𝑡{{\pi}:{[n]}\rightarrow{2^{V(G_{t})}}}. For t∈V​(T),η∈𝒞tformulae-sequence𝑡𝑉𝑇𝜂subscript𝒞𝑡t\in V(T),\eta\in\mathcal{C}_{t}, a (t,η)𝑡𝜂(t,\eta)-valid allocation π:[n]→2V​(Gt):𝜋→delimited-[]𝑛superscript2𝑉subscript𝐺𝑡{{\pi}:{[n]}\rightarrow{2^{V(G_{t})}}} and i∈[n]𝑖delimited-[]𝑛i\in[n], we call a spanning forest Hisubscript𝐻𝑖H_{i} of Gt​[π​(i)]subscript𝐺𝑡delimited-[]𝜋𝑖G_{t}[\pi(i)] that satisfies conditions (VA.2)(VA.2)(a)–(VA.2)(VA.2)(c) a (t,η,i)𝑡𝜂𝑖(t,\eta,i)-witness for π𝜋\pi.

The correctness of our DP crucially relies on the following lemma, which says that every connected component of a (t,η,i)𝑡𝜂𝑖(t,\eta,i)-witness intersects Xtsubscript𝑋𝑡X_{t}.

Let H𝐻H be a connected component of Hisubscript𝐻𝑖H_{i}.Suppose first that V​(H)∩Si≠∅𝑉𝐻subscript𝑆𝑖V(H)\cap S_{i}\neq\emptyset. Before proceeding further with our DP, let us first see how valid allocations help us prove Lemma 6.3.

Assume first that π𝜋\pi is a ((z^i)i∈[n];β)subscriptsubscript^𝑧𝑖𝑖delimited-[]𝑛𝛽((\hat{z}_{i})_{i\in[n]};\beta)-annotated allocation and vi​(π​(j))=w^i​jsubscript𝑣𝑖𝜋𝑗subscript^𝑤𝑖𝑗v_{i}(\pi(j))=\hat{w}_{ij} for every i,j∈[n]𝑖𝑗delimited-[]𝑛i,j\in[n].
